`` No ... only just that she be pretty . ''
`` I 'm rather glad of that , because I can imagine what she look like , without be hamper by fact .
I think she be very slight and small , with softly curl dark hair and big , sweet , timid brown eye , and a little wistful , pale face . ''
The girl leave their basket in Hester 's garden and spend the rest of the afternoon rambling in the wood and field surround it , discover many pretty nook and lane .
When they get hungry they have lunch in the pretty spot of all ... on the steep bank of a gurgling brook where white birch shoot up out of long feathery grass .
The girl sit down by the root and do full justice to Anne 's dainty , even the unpoetical sandwich be greatly appreciate by hearty , unspoiled appetite sharpen by all the fresh air and exercise they have enjoy .
Anne have bring glass and lemonade for her guest , but for her own part drank cold brook water from a cup fashion out of birch bark .
The cup leak , and the water taste of earth , a brook water be apt to do in spring ; but Anne think it more appropriate to the occasion than lemonade .
`` Look do you see that poem ? ''
she say suddenly , point .
`` Where ? ''
Jane and Diana star , a if expect to see Runic rhyme on the birch tree .
`` There ... down in the brook ... that old green , mossy log with the water flow over it in those smooth ripple that look a if they 'd be comb , and that single shaft of sunshine fall right athwart it , far down into the pool .
Oh , it 's the most beautiful poem I ever saw . ''
`` I should rather call it a picture , '' say Jane .
`` A poem be line and verse . ''
`` Oh dear me , no . ''
Anne shake her head with it fluffy wild cherry coronal positively .
`` The line and verse be only the outward garment of the poem and be no more really it than your ruffle and flounce be YOU , Jane .
The real poem be the soul within them ... and that beautiful bit be the soul of an unwritten poem .
It be not every day one see a soul ... even of a poem . ''
`` I wonder what a soul ... a person 's soul ... would look like , '' say Priscilla dreamily .
`` Like that , I should think , '' answer Anne , point to a radiance of sifted sunlight stream through a birch tree .
`` Only with shape and feature of course .
I like to fancy soul a be make of light .
And some be all shot through with rosy stain and quiver ... and some have a soft glitter like moonlight on the sea ... and some be pale and transparent like mist at dawn . ''
`` I read somewhere once that soul be like flower , '' say Priscilla .
`` Then your soul be a golden narcissus , '' say Anne , `` and Diana 's be like a red , red rise .
Jane 's be an apple blossom , pink and wholesome and sweet . ''
`` And your own be a white violet , with purple streak in it heart , '' finish Priscilla .
Jane whisper to Diana that she really could not understand what they be talk about .
Could she ?
The girl go home by the light of a calm golden sunset , their basket fill with narcissus blossom from Hester 's garden , some of which Anne carry to the cemetery next day and lay upon Hester 's grave .
Minstrel robin be whistle in the fir and the frog be sing in the marsh .
All the basin among the hill be brim with topaz and emerald light .
`` Well , we have have a lovely time after all , '' say Diana , a if she have hardly expect to have it when she set out .
`` It have be a truly golden day , '' say Priscilla .
`` I 'm really awfully fond of the wood myself , '' say Jane .
Anne say nothing .
She be look afar into the western sky and thinking of little Hester Gray .
XIV A Danger Averted Anne , walk home from the post office one Friday evening , be join by Mrs. Lynde , who be a usual cumber with all the care of church and state .
`` I 've just be down to Timothy Cotton 's to see if I could get Alice Louise to help me for a few day , '' she say .

But she 's sick and ca n't come .
Timothy 's sit there , too , cough and complain .
He 's be die for ten year and he 'll go on die for ten year more .
That kind ca n't even die and have do with it ... they ca n't stick to anything , even to be sick , long enough to finish it .
They 're a terrible shiftless family and what be to become of them I do n't know , but perhaps Providence do . ''
Mrs. Lynde sigh a if she rather doubt the extent of Providential knowledge on the subject .

The say `` Uncle Abe , '' it may be mention , be at least like other prophet in that he have small honor in his own country .
He be , in fact , consider in the light of a stand joke , for few of his weather prediction be ever fulfil .
Mr. Elisha Wright , who labor under the impression that he be a local wit , use to say that nobody in Avonlea ever think of look in the Charlottetown daily for weather probability .
No ; they just ask Uncle Abe what it be go to be tomorrow and expect the opposite .
Nothing daunt , Uncle Abe keep on prophesying .
`` We want to have the fair over before the election come off , '' continue Mrs. Lynde , `` for the candidate will be sure to come and spend lot of money .
The Tories be bribe right and leave , so they might as well be give a chance to spend their money honestly for once . ''
Anne be a red-hot Conservative , out of loyalty to Matthew 's memory , but she say nothing .
She know good than to get Mrs. Lynde start on politics .
She have a letter for Marilla , postmark from a town in British Columbia .
`` It 's probably from the child 's uncle , '' she say excitedly , when she get home .
`` Oh , Marilla , I wonder what he say about them . ''

A close observer might have think that she be excite also , but she would rather have die than show it .

`` He say he ca n't take the child this spring ... he 's be sick most of the winter and his wedding be put off .

We will , of course , wo n't we Marilla ? ''

Davy have improve a great deal . ''

Anne have come home from school the previous evening , to find Marilla away at an Aid meeting , Dora asleep on the kitchen sofa , and Davy in the sitting room closet , blissfully absorb the content of a jar of Marilla 's famous yellow plum preserve ... `` company jam , '' Davy call it ... which he have be forbid to touch .
He look very guilty when Anne pounce on him and whisk him out of the closet .
`` Davy Keith , do n't you know that it be very wrong of you to be eat that jam , when you be tell never to meddle with anything in THAT closet ? ''
`` Yes , I know it be wrong , '' admit Davy uncomfortably , `` but plum jam be awful nice , Anne .
I just peep in and it look so good I think I 'd take just a weeny taste .
I stick my finger in ... '' Anne groan ... `` and lick it clean .
And it be so much good than I 'd ever think that I get a spoon and just SAILED IN . ''
Anne give him such a serious lecture on the sin of steal plum jam that Davy become conscience stricken and promise with repentant kiss never to do it again .
`` Anyhow , there 'll be plenty of jam in heaven , that 's one comfort , '' he say complacently .
Anne nip a smile in the bud .
`` Perhaps there will ... if we want it , '' she say , `` But what make you think so ? ''
`` Why , it 's in the catechism , '' say Davy .
`` Oh , no , there be nothing like THAT in the catechism , Davy . ''
`` But I tell you there be , '' persist Davy .
`` It be in that question Marilla teach me last Sunday .
` Why should we love God ? '
It say , ` Because He make preserve , and redeem u . '
Preserves be just a holy way of say jam . ''
`` I must get a drink of water , '' say Anne hastily .
When she come back it cost her some time and trouble to explain to Davy that a certain comma in the say catechism question make a great deal of difference in the meaning .
`` Well , I think it be too good to be true , '' he say at last , with a sigh of disappointed conviction .
`` And besides , I do n't see when He 'd find time to make jam if it 's one endless Sabbath day , a the hymn say .
I do n't believe I want to go to heaven .
Wo n't there ever be any Saturdays in heaven , Anne ? ''
`` Yes , Saturdays , and every other kind of beautiful day .
And every day in heaven will be more beautiful than the one before it , Davy , '' assure Anne , who be rather glad that Marilla be not by to be shock .
Marilla , it be needless to say , be bring the twin up in the good old way of theology and discourage all fanciful speculation thereupon .
Davy and Dora be teach a hymn , a catechism question , and two Bible verse every Sunday .
Dora learn meekly and recite like a little machine , with perhaps a much understanding or interest a if she be one .
Davy , on the contrary , have a lively curiosity , and frequently ask question which make Marilla tremble for his fate .
`` Chester Sloane say we 'll do nothing all the time in heaven but walk around in white dress and play on harp ; and he say he hop he wo n't have to go till he 's an old man , 'cause maybe he 'll like it good then .
And he think it will be horrid to wear dress and I think so too .
Why ca n't men angels wear trouser , Anne ?
Chester Sloane be interested in those thing , 'cause they 're go to make a minister of him .
He 's get to be a minister 'cause his grandmother leave the money to send him to college and he ca n't have it unless he be a minister .
She think a minister be such a ` spectable thing to have in a family .

He be an agent for agricultural implement and a prominent personage in matter political .

Soon Judson Parker overtake her .
`` Have a lift , Anne ? ''
he inquire genially .

`` And ... and ... you need n't mention that little conversation of mine with Jerry . ''

`` I do n't suppose you would .
Of course , I be only string Jerry ... he think he 's so all-fired cute and smart .

XV The Beginning of Vacation Anne lock the schoolhouse door on a still , yellow evening , when the wind be purr in the spruce around the playground , and the shadow be long and lazy by the edge of the wood .
She drop the key into her pocket with a sigh of satisfaction .
The school year be end , she have be reengaged for the next , with many expression of satisfaction .
... only Mr. Harmon Andrews tell her she ought to use the strap oftener ... and two delightful month of a well-earned vacation beckon her invitingly .
Anne felt at peace with the world and herself a she walk down the hill with her basket of flower in her hand .
Since the early mayflower Anne have never miss her weekly pilgrimage to Matthew 's grave .
Everyone else in Avonlea , except Marilla , have already forget quiet , shy , unimportant Matthew Cuthbert ; but his memory be still green in Anne 's heart and always would be .
She could never forget the kind old man who have be the first to give her the love and sympathy her starve childhood have crave .
At the foot of the hill a boy be sit on the fence in the shadow of the spruce ... a boy with big , dreamy eye and a beautiful , sensitive face .
He swing down and join Anne , smile ; but there be trace of tear on his cheek .
`` I think I 'd wait for you , teacher , because I know you be go to the graveyard , '' he say , slip his hand into hers .
`` I 'm go there , too ... I 'm take this bouquet of geranium to put on Grandpa Irving 's grave for grandma .
And look , teacher , I 'm go to put this bunch of white rose beside Grandpa 's grave in memory of my little mother ... because I ca n't go to her grave to put it there .
But do n't you think she 'll know all about it , just the same ? ''
`` Yes , I be sure she will , Paul . ''
`` You see , teacher , it 's just three year today since my little mother die .
It 's such a long , long time but it hurt just as much a ever ... and I miss her just as much a ever .
Sometimes it seem to me that I just ca n't bear it , it hurt so . ''
Paul 's voice quiver and his lip tremble .
He look down at his rose , hop that his teacher would not notice the tear in his eye .
`` And yet , '' say Anne , very softly , `` you would n't want it to stop hurting ... you would n't want to forget your little mother even if you could . ''
`` No , indeed , I would n't ... that 's just the way I feel .
You 're so good at understand , teacher .
Nobody else understand so well ... not even grandma , although she 's so good to me .
Father understood pretty well , but still I could n't talk much to him about mother , because it make him feel so bad .
When he put his hand over his face I always know it be time to stop .
Poor father , he must be dreadfully lonesome without me ; but you see he have nobody but a housekeeper now and he think housekeeper be no good to bring up little boy , especially when he have to be away from home so much on business .
Grandmothers be well , next to mother .
Someday , when I 'm bring up , I 'll go back to father and we 're never go to be part again . ''
Paul have talk so much to Anne about his mother and father that she felt a if she have know them .
She think his mother must have be very like what he be himself , in temperament and disposition ; and she have an idea that Stephen Irving be a rather reserve man with a deep and tender nature which he keep hidden scrupulously from the world .
`` Father 's not very easy to get acquaint with , '' Paul have say once .
`` I never get really acquaint with him until after my little mother die .
But he 's splendid when you do get to know him .
I love him the best in all the world , and Grandma Irving next , and then you , teacher .
I 'd love you next to father if it be n't my DUTY to love Grandma Irving best , because she 's do so much for me .
YOU know , teacher .
I wish she would leave the lamp in my room till I go to sleep , though .
She take it right out as soon a she tuck me up because she say I must n't be a coward .
I 'm NOT scar , but I 'd RATHER have the light .
My little mother use always to sit beside me and hold my hand till I go to sleep .
I expect she spoil me .
Mothers do sometimes , you know . ''
No , Anne do not know this , although she might imagine it .
She think sadly of HER `` little mother , '' the mother who have think her so `` perfectly beautiful '' and who have die so long ago and be bury beside her boyish husband in that unvisited grave far away .
Anne could not remember her mother and for this reason she almost envy Paul .
`` My birthday be next week , '' say Paul , a they walk up the long red hill , bask in the June sunshine , `` and father write me that he be send me something that he think I 'll like good than anything else he could send .
I believe it have come already , for Grandma be keep the bookcase drawer lock and that be something new .
And when I ask her why , she just look mysterious and say little boy must n't be too curious .
It 's very exciting to have a birthday , be n't it ?
I 'll be eleven .
You 'd never think it to look at me , would you ?
Grandma say I 'm very small for my age and that it 's all because I do n't eat enough porridge .
I do my very best , but Grandma give such generous plateful ... there 's nothing mean about Grandma , I can tell you .
Ever since you and I have that talk about pray go home from Sunday School that day , teacher ... when you say we ought to pray about all our difficulty ... I 've pray every night that God would give me enough grace to enable me to eat every bit of my porridge in the morning .
But I 've never be able to do it yet , and whether it 's because I have too little grace or too much porridge I really ca n't decide .
Grandma say father be bring up on porridge , and it certainly do work well in his case , for you ought to see the shoulder he have .
But sometimes , '' conclude Paul with a sigh and a meditative air `` I really think porridge will be the death of me . ''
Anne permit herself a smile , since Paul be not look at her .
All Avonlea know that old Mrs. Irving be bring her grandson up in accordance with the good , old-fashioned method of diet and moral .
`` Let u hope not , dear , '' she say cheerfully .
`` How be your rock people come on ?
Does the old Twin still continue to behave himself ? ''
`` He HAS to , '' say Paul emphatically .
`` He know I wo n't associate with him if he do n't .
He be really full of wickedness , I think . ''
`` And have Nora find out about the Golden Lady yet ? ''
`` No ; but I think she suspect .
I 'm almost sure she watch me the last time I go to the cave .
I do n't mind if she find out ... it be only for HER sake I do n't want her to ... so that her feeling wo n't be hurt .
But if she be DETERMINED to have her feeling hurt it ca n't be help . ''
`` If I be to go to the shore some night with you do you think I could see your rock people too ? ''
Paul shake his head gravely .
`` No , I do n't think you could see MY rock people .
I 'm the only person who can see them .
But you could see rock people of your own .
You 're one of the kind that can .
We 're both that kind .
YOU know , teacher , '' he add , squeeze her hand chummily .
`` Is n't it splendid to be that kind , teacher ? ''
`` Splendid , '' Anne agree , gray shin eye look down into blue shin one .
Anne and Paul both know `` How fair the realm Imagination open to the view , '' and both know the way to that happy land .
There the rise of joy bloom immortal by dale and stream ; cloud never darken the sunny sky ; sweet bell never jangle out of tune ; and kindred spirit abound .
The knowledge of that land 's geography ... `` east o ' the sun , west o ' the moon '' ... be priceless lore , not to be buy in any market place .
It must be the gift of the good fairy at birth and the year can never deface it or take it away .
It be well to possess it , live in a garret , than to be the inhabitant of palace without it .
The Avonlea graveyard be a yet the grass-grown solitude it have always be .
To be sure , the Improvers have an eye on it , and Priscilla Grant have read a paper on cemetery before the last meeting of the Society .
At some future time the Improvers mean to have the lichened , wayward old board fence replace by a neat wire railing , the grass mown and the leaning monument straighten up .
Anne put on Matthew 's grave the flower she have bring for it , and then go over to the little poplar shade corner where Hester Gray sleep .
Ever since the day of the spring picnic Anne have put flower on Hester 's grave when she visit Matthew 's .
The evening before she have make a pilgrimage back to the little deserted garden in the wood and bring therefrom some of Hester 's own white rose .
`` I think you would like them good than any others , dear , '' she say softly .
Anne be still sit there when a shadow fell over the grass and she look up to see Mrs. Allan .
They walk home together .
Mrs. Allan 's face be not the face of the girlbride whom the minister have bring to Avonlea five year before .
It have lose some of it bloom and youthful curve , and there be fine , patient line about eye and mouth .
A tiny grave in that very cemetery account for some of them ; and some new one have come during the recent illness , now happily over , of her little son .
But Mrs. Allan 's dimple be a sweet and sudden a ever , her eye a clear and bright and true ; and what her face lack of girlish beauty be now more than atone for in added tenderness and strength .
`` I suppose you be look forward to your vacation , Anne ? ''
she say , a they leave the graveyard .
Anne nod .
`` Yes ... I could roll the word a a sweet morsel under my tongue .
I think the summer be go to be lovely .
For one thing , Mrs. Morgan be come to the Island in July and Priscilla be go to bring her up .
I feel one of my old ` thrill ' at the mere thought . ''
`` I hope you 'll have a good time , Anne .
You 've work very hard this past year and you have succeed . ''
`` Oh , I do n't know .
I 've come so far short in so many thing .
I have n't do what I mean to do when I begin to teach last fall .
I have n't live up to my ideal . ''
`` None of u ever do , '' say Mrs. Allan with a sigh .
`` But then , Anne , you know what Lowell say , ` Not failure but low aim be crime . '
We must have ideal and try to live up to them , even if we never quite succeed .
Life would be a sorry business without them .
With them it 's grand and great .
Hold fast to your ideal , Anne . ''
`` I shall try .
But I have to let go most of my theory , '' say Anne , laugh a little .
`` I have the most beautiful set of theory you ever know when I start out a a schoolma'am , but every one of them have fail me at some pinch or another . ''
`` Even the theory on corporal punishment , '' tease Mrs. Allan .
But Anne flush .
`` I shall never forgive myself for whip Anthony . ''
`` Nonsense , dear , he deserve it .
And it agree with him .
You have have no trouble with him since and he have come to think there 's nobody like you .
Your kindness win his love after the idea that a ` girl be no good ' be root out of his stubborn mind . ''
`` He may have deserve it , but that be not the point .
If I have calmly and deliberately decide to whip him because I think it a just punishment for him I would not feel over it a I do .
But the truth be , Mrs. Allan , that I just fly into a temper and whip him because of that .
I be n't think whether it be just or unjust ... even if he have n't deserve it I 'd have do it just the same .
That be what humiliate me . ''
`` Well , we all make mistake , dear , so just put it behind you .
We should regret our mistake and learn from them , but never carry them forward into the future with u .
There go Gilbert Blythe on his wheel ... home for his vacation too , I suppose .
How be you and he get on with your study ? ''
`` Pretty well .
We plan to finish the Virgil tonight ... there be only twenty line to do .
Then we be not go to study any more until September . ''
`` Do you think you will ever get to college ? ''
`` Oh , I do n't know . ''
Anne look dreamily afar to the opal-tinted horizon .
`` Marilla 's eye will never be much good than they be now , although we be so thankful to think that they will not get bad .
And then there be the twin ... somehow I do n't believe their uncle will ever really send for them .
Perhaps college may be around the bend in the road , but I have n't get to the bend yet and I do n't think much about it l I might grow discontent . ''
`` Well , I should like to see you go to college , Anne ; but if you never do , do n't be discontent about it .
We make our own life wherever we be , after all ... college can only help u to do it more easily .
They be broad or narrow accord to what we put into them , not what we get out .
Life be rich and full here ... everywhere ... if we can only learn how to open our whole heart to it richness and fulness . ''
`` I think I understand what you mean , '' say Anne thoughtfully , `` and I know I have so much to feel thankful for ... oh , so much ... my work , and Paul Irving , and the dear twin , and all my friend .
Do you know , Mrs. Allan , I 'm so thankful for friendship .
It beautify life so much . ''
`` True friendship be a very helpful thing indeed , '' say Mrs. Allan , `` and we should have a very high ideal of it , and never sully it by any failure in truth and sincerity .
I fear the name of friendship be often degrade to a kind of intimacy that have nothing of real friendship in it . ''
`` Yes ... like Gertie Pye 's and Julia Bell 's .
They be very intimate and go everywhere together ; but Gertie be always say nasty thing of Julia behind her back and everybody think she be jealous of her because she be always so pleased when anybody criticize Julia .
I think it be desecration to call that friendship .
If we have friend we should look only for the best in them and give them the best that be in u , do n't you think ?
Then friendship would be the most beautiful thing in the world . ''
`` Friendship IS very beautiful , '' smile Mrs. Allan , `` but some day ... '' Then she pause abruptly .
In the delicate , white-browed face beside her , with it candid eye and mobile feature , there be still far more of the child than of the woman .
Anne 's heart so far harbor only dream of friendship and ambition , and Mrs. Allan do not wish to brush the bloom from her sweet unconsciousness .
So she leave her sentence for the future year to finish .
XVI The Substance of Things Hoped For `` Anne , '' say Davy appealingly , scramble up on the shiny , leather-covered sofa in the Green Gables kitchen , where Anne sit , read a letter , `` Anne , I 'm AWFUL hungry .
You 've no idea . ''
`` I 'll get you a piece of bread and butter in a minute , '' say Anne absently .
Her letter evidently contain some exciting news , for her cheek be as pink a the rose on the big bush outside , and her eye be as starry a only Anne 's eye could be .
`` But I ai n't bread and butter hungry , '' say Davy in a disgusted tone .
`` I 'm plum cake hungry . ''
`` Oh , '' laugh Anne , lay down her letter and put her arm about Davy to give him a squeeze , `` that 's a kind of hunger that can be endure very comfortably , Davy-boy .
You know it 's one of Marilla 's rule that you ca n't have anything but bread and butter between meal . ''
`` Well , gim me a piece then ... please . ''
Davy have be at last taught to say `` please , '' but he generally tack it on a an afterthought .
He look with approval at the generous slice Anne presently bring to him .
`` You always put such a nice lot of butter on it , Anne .
Marilla spread it pretty thin .
It slip down a lot easy when there 's plenty of butter . ''
The slice `` slip down '' with tolerable ease , judge from it rapid disappearance .
Davy slide head first off the sofa , turn a double somersault on the rug , and then sit up and announce decidedly , `` Anne , I 've make up my mind about heaven .
I do n't want to go there . ''
`` Why not ? ''
ask Anne gravely .
`` Cause heaven be in Simon Fletcher 's garret , and I do n't like Simon Fletcher . ''
`` Heaven in ... Simon Fletcher 's garret ! ''
gasp Anne , too amazed even to laugh .
`` Davy Keith , whatever put such an extraordinary idea into your head ? ''
`` Milty Boulter say that 's where it be .
It be last Sunday in Sunday School .
The lesson be about Elijah and Elisha , and I up and ask Miss Rogerson where heaven be .
Miss Rogerson look awful offend .
She be cross anyhow , because when she 'd ask u what Elijah leave Elisha when he go to heaven Milty Boulter say , ` His old clo'es , ' and u fellows all laugh before we think .
I wish you could think first and do thing afterwards , 'cause then you would n't do them .
But Milty do n't mean to be disrespeckful .
He just could n't think of the name of the thing .
Miss Rogerson say heaven be where God be and I be n't to ask question like that .
Milty nudge me and say in a whisper , ` Heaven 's in Uncle Simon 's garret and I 'll esplain about it on the road home . '
So when we be come home he esplained .
Milty 's a great hand at esplaining thing .
Even if he do n't know anything about a thing he 'll make up a lot of stuff and so you get it esplained all the same .
His mother be Mrs. Simon 's sister and he go with her to the funeral when his cousin , Jane Ellen , die .
The minister say she 'd go to heaven , though Milty say she be lie right before them in the coffin .
But he s ` pose they carry the coffin to the garret afterwards .
Well , when Milty and his mother go upstairs after it be all over to get her bonnet he ask her where heaven be that Jane Ellen have go to , and she point right to the ceiling and say , ` Up there . '
Milty know there be n't anything but the garret over the ceiling , so that 's how HE find out .
And he 's be awful scared to go to his Uncle Simon 's ever since . ''
Anne take Davy on her knee and do her best to straighten out this theological tangle also .
She be much good fit for the task than Marilla , for she remember her own childhood and have an instinctive understanding of the curious idea that seven-year-olds sometimes get about matter that be , of course , very plain and simple to grow up people .
She have just succeed in convince Davy that heaven be NOT in Simon Fletcher 's garret when Marilla come in from the garden , where she and Dora have be pick pea .
Dora be an industrious little soul and never happy than when `` help '' in various small task suit to her chubby finger .
She feed chicken , pick up chip , wipe dish , and run errand galore .
She be neat , faithful and observant ; she never have to be tell how to do a thing twice and never forget any of her little duty .
Davy , on the other hand , be rather heedless and forgetful ; but he have the born knack of win love , and even yet Anne and Marilla like him the good .
While Dora proudly shell the pea and Davy make boat of the pod , with mast of match and sail of paper , Anne tell Marilla about the wonderful content of her letter .
`` Oh , Marilla , what do you think ?
I 've have a letter from Priscilla and she say that Mrs. Morgan be on the Island , and that if it be fine Thursday they be go to drive up to Avonlea and will reach here about twelve .
They will spend the afternoon with u and go to the hotel at White Sands in the evening , because some of Mrs. Morgan 's American friend be stay there .
Oh , Marilla , be n't it wonderful ?
I can hardly believe I 'm not dream . ''
`` I daresay Mrs. Morgan be a lot like other people , '' say Marilla drily , although she do feel a trifle excite herself .
Mrs. Morgan be a famous woman and a visit from her be no commonplace occurrence .
`` They 'll be here to dinner , then ? ''
`` Yes ; and oh , Marilla , may I cook every bit of the dinner myself ?
I want to feel that I can do something for the author of ` The Rosebud Garden , ' if it be only to cook a dinner for her .
You wo n't mind , will you ? ''
`` Goodness , I 'm not so fond of stew over a hot fire in July that it would vex me very much to have someone else do it .
You 're quite welcome to the job . ''
`` Oh , thank you , '' say Anne , a if Marilla have just confer a tremendous favor , `` I 'll make out the menu this very night . ''
`` You 'd better not try to put on too much style , '' warn Marilla , a little alarmed by the high-flown sound of ` menu . '
`` You 'll likely come to grief if you do . ''
`` Oh , I 'm not go to put on any ` style , ' if you mean try to do or have thing we do n't usually have on festal occasion , '' assure Anne .
`` That would be affectation , and , although I know I have n't as much sense and steadiness a a girl of seventeen and a schoolteacher ought to have , I 'm not so silly a THAT .
But I want to have everything a nice and dainty a possible .
Davy-boy , do n't leave those peapods on the back stair ... someone might slip on them .
I 'll have a light soup to begin with ... you know I can make lovely cream-of-onion soup ... and then a couple of roast fowl .
I 'll have the two white rooster .
I have real affection for those rooster and they 've be pet ever since the gray hen hatch out just the two of them ... little ball of yellow down .
But I know they would have to be sacrifice sometime , and surely there could n't be a worthy occasion than this .
But oh , Marilla , I can not kill them ... not even for Mrs. Morgan 's sake .
I 'll have to ask John Henry Carter to come over and do it for me . ''
`` I 'll do it , '' volunteer Davy , `` if Marilla 'll hold them by the leg , 'cause I guess it 'd take both my hand to manage the axe .
It 's awful jolly fun to see them hop about after their head be cut off . ''
`` Then I 'll have pea and bean and cream potato and a lettuce salad , for vegetable , '' resume Anne , `` and for dessert , lemon pie with whipped cream , and coffee and cheese and lady finger .
I 'll make the pie and lady finger tomorrow and do up my white muslin dress .
And I must tell Diana tonight , for she 'll want to do up hers .
Mrs. Morgan 's heroine be nearly always dress in white muslin , and Diana and I have always resolve that that be what we would wear if we ever meet her .
It will be such a delicate compliment , do n't you think ?
Davy , dear , you must n't poke peapods into the crack of the floor .
I must ask Mr. and Mrs. Allan and Miss Stacy to dinner , too , for they 're all very anxious to meet Mrs. Morgan .
It 's so fortunate she 's come while Miss Stacy be here .
Davy dear , do n't sail the peapods in the water bucket ... go out to the trough .
Oh , I do hope it will be fine Thursday , and I think it will , for Uncle Abe say last night when he call at Mr. Harrison 's , that it be go to rain most of this week . ''
`` That 's a good sign , '' agree Marilla .
Anne run across to Orchard Slope that even to tell the news to Diana , who be also very much excite over it , and they discuss the matter in the hammock swung under the big willow in the Barry garden .
`` Oh , Anne , may n't I help you cook the dinner ? ''
implored Diana .
`` You know I can make splendid lettuce salad . ''
`` Indeed you , may '' say Anne unselfishly .
`` And I shall want you to help me decorate too .
I mean to have the parlor simply a BOWER of blossom ... and the dining table be to be adorn with wild rose .
Oh , I do hope everything will go smoothly .
Mrs. Morgan 's heroine NEVER get into scrape or be take at a disadvantage , and they be always so selfpossessed and such good housekeeper .
They seem to be BORN good housekeeper .
You remember that Gertrude in ` Edgewood Days ' kept house for her father when she be only eight year old .
When I be eight year old I hardly know how to do a thing except bring up child .

I 've imagine it all out a dozen different way ... what she 'll look like , and what she 'll say , and what I 'll say .
And I 'm so anxious about my nose .
There be seven freckle on it , a you can see .
They come at the A.V.I S. picnic , when I go around in the sun without my hat .
I suppose it 's ungrateful of me to worry over them , when I should be thankful they 're not spread all over my face a they once be ; but I do wish they have n't come ... all Mrs. Morgan 's heroine have such perfect complexion .
I ca n't recall a freckled one among them . ''
`` Yours be not very noticeable , '' comfort Diana .
`` Try a little lemon juice on them tonight . ''
The next day Anne make her pie and lady finger , do up her muslin dress , and swept and dust every room in the house ... a quite unnecessary proceeding , for Green Gables be , a usual , in the apple pie order dear to Marilla 's heart .
But Anne felt that a fleck of dust would be a desecration in a house that be to be honor by a visit from Charlotte E. Morgan .
She even clean out the `` catch-all '' closet under the stair , although there be not the remote possibility of Mrs. Morgan 's see it interior .
`` But I want to FEEL that it be in perfect order , even if she be n't to see it , '' Anne tell Marilla .
`` You know , in her book ` Golden Keys , ' she make her two heroine Alice and Louisa take for their motto that verse of Longfellow 's , ` In the elder day of art Builders work with great care Each minute and unseen part , For the god see everywhere , ' and so they always keep their cellar stair scrub and never forget to sweep under the bed .
I should have a guilty conscience if I think this closet be in disorder when Mrs. Morgan be in the house .
Ever since we read ` Golden Keys , ' last April , Diana and I have take that verse for our motto too . ''
That night John Henry Carter and Davy between them contrive to execute the two white rooster , and Anne dress them , the usually distasteful task glorify in her eye by the destination of the plump bird .
`` I do n't like pick fowl , '' she tell Marilla , `` but be n't it fortunate we do n't have to put our soul into what our hand may be do ?
I 've be pick chicken with my hand but in imagination I 've be roam the Milky Way . ''
`` I think you 'd scatter more feather over the floor than usual , '' remark Marilla .
Then Anne put Davy to bed and make him promise that he would behave perfectly the next day .
`` If I 'm as good a good can be all day tomorrow will you let me be just as bad a I like all the next day ? ''
ask Davy .
`` I could n't do that , '' say Anne discreetly , `` but I 'll take you and Dora for a row in the flat right to the bottom of the pond , and we 'll go ashore on the sandhills and have a picnic . ''
`` It 's a bargain , '' say Davy .
`` I 'll be good , you bet .
I mean to go over to Mr. Harrison 's and fire pea from my new popgun at Ginger but another day 'll do as well .
I espect it will be just like Sunday , but a picnic at the shore 'll make up for THAT . ''
XVII A Chapter of Accidents Anne wake three time in the night and make pilgrimage to her window to make sure that Uncle Abe 's prediction be not come true .
Finally the morning dawn pearly and lustrous in a sky full of silver sheen and radiance , and the wonderful day have arrive .
Diana appear soon after breakfast , with a basket of flower over one arm and HER muslin dress over the other ... for it would not do to don it until all the dinner preparation be complete .
Meanwhile she wear her afternoon pink print and a lawn apron fearfully and wonderfully ruffle and frilled ; and very neat and pretty and rosy she be .
`` You look simply sweet , '' say Anne admiringly .
Diana sigh .
`` But I 've have to let out every one of my dress AGAIN .
I weigh four pound more than I do in July .
Anne , WHERE will this end ?
Mrs. Morgan 's heroine be all tall and slender . ''

`` Mrs. Allan say that whenever we think of anything that be a trial to u we should also think of something nice that we can set over against it .

There may have be two happy and more excited girl somewhere in Canada or the United States at that moment , but I doubt it .

The table be set in the sitting room , with Marilla 's fine linen and the best china , glass , and silver .

Then the girl trip out to the kitchen , which be fill with appetizing odor emanate from the oven , where the chicken be already sizzle splendidly .

And what about Davy all this time ?
Was he redeem his promise to be good ?
He be , indeed .

wail Davy .

Nobody do , I imagine . ''

XVIII An Adventure on the Tory Road `` Anne , '' say Davy , sit up in bed and prop his chin on his hand , `` Anne , where be sleep ?
People go to sleep every night , and of course I know it 's the place where I do the thing I dream , but I want to know WHERE it be and how I get there and back without know anything about it ... and in my nighty too .
Where be it ? ''
Anne be kneel at the west gable window watch the sunset sky that be like a great flower with petal of crocus and a heart of fiery yellow .
She turn her head at Davy 's question and answer dreamily , '' ` Over the mountain of the moon , Down the valley of the shadow . ' ''
Paul Irving would have know the meaning of this , or make a mean out of it for himself , if he do n't ; but practical Davy , who , a Anne often despairingly remark , have n't a particle of imagination , be only puzzled and disgust .
`` Anne , I believe you 're just talk nonsense . ''
`` Of course , I be , dear boy .
Do n't you know that it be only very foolish folk who talk sense all the time ? ''
`` Well , I think you might give a sensible answer when I ask a sensible question , '' say Davy in an injured tone .
`` Oh , you be too little to understand , '' say Anne .
But she felt rather ashamed of say it ; for have she not , in keen remembrance of many similar snub administer in her own early year , solemnly vow that she would never tell any child it be too little to understand ?
Yet here she be do it ... so wide sometimes be the gulf between theory and practice .
`` Well , I 'm do my best to grow , '' say Davy , `` but it 's a thing you ca n't hurry much .
If Marilla be n't so stingy with her jam I believe I 'd grow a lot faster . ''
`` Marilla be not stingy , Davy , '' say Anne severely .
`` It be very ungrateful of you to say such a thing . ''
`` There 's another word that mean the same thing and sound a lot well , but I do n't just remember it , '' say Davy , frown intently .
`` I hear Marilla say she be it , herself , the other day . ''
`` If you mean ECONOMICAL , it 's a VERY different thing from be stingy .
It be an excellent trait in a person if she be economical .
If Marilla have be stingy she would n't have take you and Dora when your mother die .
Would you have like to live with Mrs. Wiggins ? ''
`` You just bet I would n't ! ''
Davy be emphatic on that point .
`` Nor I do n't want to go out to Uncle Richard neither .
I 'd far rather live here , even if Marilla be that long-tailed word when it come to jam , 'cause YOU 'RE here , Anne .
Say , Anne , wo n't you tell me a story ` fore I go to sleep ?
I do n't want a fairy story .
They 're all right for girl , I s ` pose , but I want something excite ... lot of kill and shoot in it , and a house on fire , and i n'trusting thing like that . ''
Fortunately for Anne , Marilla call out at this moment from her room .
`` Anne , Diana 's signaling at a great rate .
You 'd better see what she want . ''
Anne run to the east gable and saw flash of light come through the twilight from Diana 's window in group of five , which mean , accord to their old childish code , `` Come over at once for I have something important to reveal . ''
Anne throw her white shawl over her head and hasten through the Haunted Wood and across Mr. Bell 's pasture corner to Orchard Slope .
`` I 've good news for you , Anne , '' say Diana .
`` Mother and I have just get home from Carmody , and I saw Mary Sentner from Spencer vale in Mr. Blair 's store .
She say the old Copp girl on the Tory Road have a willow-ware platter and she think it 's exactly like the one we have at the supper .
She say they 'll likely sell it , for Martha Copp have never be know to keep anything she COULD sell ; but if they wo n't there 's a platter at Wesley Keyson 's at Spencervale and she know they 'd sell it , but she be n't sure it 's just the same kind a Aunt Josephine 's . ''
`` I 'll go right over to Spencervale after it tomorrow , '' say Anne resolutely , `` and you must come with me .
It will be such a weight off my mind , for I have to go to town day after tomorrow and how can I face your Aunt Josephine without a willow-ware platter ?
It would be even bad than the time I have to confess about jump on the spare room bed . ''
Both girl laugh over the old memory ... concern which , if any of my reader be ignorant and curious , I must refer them to Anne 's early history .
The next afternoon the girl fare forth on their platter hunt expedition .
It be ten mile to Spencervale and the day be not especially pleasant for travel .
It be very warm and windless , and the dust on the road be such a might have be expect after six week of dry weather .
`` Oh , I do wish it would rain soon , '' sigh Anne .
`` Everything be so parch up .
The poor field just seem pitiful to me and the tree seem to be stretch out their hand plead for rain .
As for my garden , it hurt me every time I go into it .
I suppose I should n't complain about a garden when the farmer ' crop be suffer so .
Mr. Harrison say his pasture be so scorch up that his poor cow can hardly get a bite to eat and he feel guilty of cruelty to animals every time he meet their eye . ''
After a wearisome drive the girl reach Spencervale and turn down the `` Tory '' Road ... a green , solitary highway where the strip of grass between the wheel track bore evidence to lack of travel .
Along most of it extent it be line with thick-set young spruce crowd down to the roadway , with here and there a break where the back field of a Spencervale farm come out to the fence or an expanse of stump be aflame with fireweed and goldenrod .
`` Why be it call the Tory Road ? ''
ask Anne .
`` Mr. Allan say it be on the principle of call a place a grove because there be no tree in it , '' say Diana , `` for nobody live along the road except the Copp girl and old Martin Bovyer at the further end , who be a Liberal .
The Tory government run the road through when they be in power just to show they be do something . ''
Diana 's father be a Liberal , for which reason she and Anne never discuss politics .
Green Gables folk have always be Conservatives .
Finally the girl come to the old Copp homestead ... a place of such exceed external neatness that even Green Gables would have suffer by contrast .
The house be a very old-fashioned one , situate on a slope , which fact have necessitate the building of a stone basement under one end .
The house and out-buildings be all whitewash to a condition of blind perfection and not a weed be visible in the prim kitchen garden surround by it white paling .
`` The shade be all down , '' say Diana ruefully .
`` I believe that nobody be home . ''
This prove to be the case .
The girl look at each other in perplexity .
`` I do n't know what to do , '' say Anne .
`` If I be sure the platter be the right kind I would not mind wait until they come home .
But if it be n't it may be too late to go to Wesley Keyson 's afterward . ''
Diana look at a certain little square window over the basement .
`` That be the pantry window , I feel sure , '' she say , `` because this house be just like Uncle Charles ' at Newbridge , and that be their pantry window .
The shade be n't down , so if we climb up on the roof of that little house we could look into the pantry and might be able to see the platter .
Do you think it would be any harm ? ''
`` No , I do n't think so , '' decide Anne , after due reflection , `` since our motive be not idle curiosity . ''
This important point of ethic be settle , Anne prepare to mount the aforesaid `` little house , '' a construction of lathe , with a peaked roof , which have in time past serve a a habitation for duck .
The Copp girl have give up keep duck ... `` because they be such untidy bird '' .
.
.
and the house have not be in use for some year , save a an abode of correction for set hen .
Although scrupulously whitewash it have become somewhat shaky , and Anne felt rather dubious a she scramble up from the vantage point of a keg place on a box .
`` I 'm afraid it wo n't bear my weight , '' she say a she gingerly step on the roof .
`` Lean on the window sill , '' advise Diana , and Anne accordingly lean .
Much to her delight , she saw , a she peer through the pane , a willow-ware platter , exactly such a she be in quest of , on the shelf in front of the window .
So much she saw before the catastrophe come .
In her joy Anne forget the precarious nature of her footing , incautiously cease to lean on the window sill , give an impulsive little hop of pleasure ... and the next moment she have crash through the roof up to her armpit , and there she hang , quite unable to extricate herself .
Diana dash into the duck house and , seize her unfortunate friend by the waist , try to draw her down .
`` Ow ... do n't , '' shriek poor Anne .
`` There be some long splinter stick into me .
See if you can put something under my foot ... then perhaps I can draw myself up . ''
Diana hastily drag in the previously mention keg and Anne find that it be just sufficiently high to furnish a secure rest place for her foot .
But she could not release herself .
`` Could I pull you out if I crawl up ? ''
suggest Diana .
Anne shake her head hopelessly .
`` No ... the splinter hurt too badly .
If you can find an axe you might chop me out , though .
Oh dear , I do really begin to believe that I be bear under an ill-omened star . ''
Diana search faithfully but no axe be to be find .
`` I 'll have to go for help , '' she say , return to the prisoner .
`` No , indeed , you wo n't , '' say Anne vehemently .
`` If you do the story of this will get out everywhere and I shall be ashamed to show my face .
No , we must just wait until the Copp girl come home and bind them to secrecy .
They 'll know where the axe be and get me out .
I 'm not uncomfortable , as long a I keep perfectly still ... not uncomfortable in BODY I mean .
I wonder what the Copp girl value this house at .
I shall have to pay for the damage I 've do , but I would n't mind that if I be only sure they would understand my motive in peep in at their pantry window .
My sole comfort be that the platter be just the kind I want and if Miss Copp will only sell it to me I shall be resign to what have happen . ''
`` What if the Copp girl do n't come home until after night ... or till tomorrow ? ''
suggest Diana .
`` If they 're not back by sunset you 'll have to go for other assistance , I suppose , '' say Anne reluctantly , `` but you must n't go until you really have to .
Oh dear , this be a dreadful predicament .
I would n't mind my misfortune so much if they be romantic , a Mrs. Morgan 's heroine ' always be , but they be always just simply ridiculous .
Fancy what the Copp girl will think when they drive into their yard and see a girl 's head and shoulder stick out of the roof of one of their outhouse .
Listen ... be that a wagon ?
No , Diana , I believe it be thunder . ''
Thunder it be undoubtedly , and Diana , have make a hasty pilgrimage around the house , return to announce that a very black cloud be rise rapidly in the northwest .
`` I believe we 're go to have a heavy thunder-shower , '' she exclaim in dismay , `` Oh , Anne , what will we do ? ''
`` We must prepare for it , '' say Anne tranquilly .
A thunderstorm seem a trifle in comparison with what have already happen .
`` You 'd better drive the horse and buggy into that open shed .
Fortunately my parasol be in the buggy .
Here ... take my hat with you .
Marilla tell me I be a goose to put on my best hat to come to the Tory Road and she be right , a she always be . ''
Diana untie the pony and drive into the shed , just a the first heavy drop of rain fell .
There she sit and watch the result downpour , which be so thick and heavy that she could hardly see Anne through it , hold the parasol bravely over her bare head .
There be not a great deal of thunder , but for the best part of an hour the rain come merrily down .
Occasionally Anne slant back her parasol and wave an encouraging hand to her friend ; But conversation at that distance be quite out of the question .
Finally the rain cease , the sun come out , and Diana venture across the puddle of the yard .
`` Did you get very wet ? ''
she ask anxiously .
`` Oh , no , '' return Anne cheerfully .
`` My head and shoulder be quite dry and my skirt be only a little damp where the rain beat through the lathe .
Do n't pity me , Diana , for I have n't mind it at all .
I keep think how much good the rain will do and how glad my garden must be for it , and imagine what the flower and bud would think when the drop begin to fall .
I imagine out a most interesting dialogue between the aster and the sweet pea and the wild canary in the lilac bush and the guardian spirit of the garden .
When I go home I mean to write it down .
I wish I have a pencil and paper to do it now , because I daresay I 'll forget the best part before I reach home . ''
Diana the faithful have a pencil and discover a sheet of wrap paper in the box of the buggy .
Anne fold up her drip parasol , put on her hat , spread the wrap paper on a shingle Diana hand up , and write out her garden idyl under condition that could hardly be consider a favorable to literature .
Nevertheless , the result be quite pretty , and Diana be `` enrapture '' when Anne read it to her .
`` Oh , Anne , it 's sweet ... just sweet .
DO send it to the ` Canadian Woman . ' ''
Anne shake her head .
`` Oh , no , it would n't be suitable at all .
There be no PLOT in it , you see .
It 's just a string of fancy .
I like write such thing , but of course nothing of the sort would ever do for publication , for editor insist on plot , so Priscilla say .
Oh , there 's Miss Sarah Copp now .
PLEASE , Diana , go and explain . ''
Miss Sarah Copp be a small person , garb in shabby black , with a hat chosen less for vain adornment than for quality that would wear well .
She look as amaze a might be expect on see the curious tableau in her yard , but when she hear Diana 's explanation she be all sympathy .
She hurriedly unlock the back door , produce the axe , and with a few skillfull blow set Anne free .
The latter , somewhat tired and stiff , duck down into the interior of her prison and thankfully emerge into liberty once more .
`` Miss Copp , '' she say earnestly .
`` I assure you I look into your pantry window only to discover if you have a willow-ware platter .
I do n't see anything else -- I do n't LOOK for anything else . ''
`` Bless you , that 's all right , '' say Miss Sarah amiably .
`` You need n't worry -- there 's no harm do .
Thank goodness , we Copps keep our pantry presentable at all time and do n't care who see into them .
As for that old duckhouse , I 'm glad it 's smash , for maybe now Martha will agree to have it take down .
She never would before for fear it might come in handy sometime and I 've have to whitewash it every spring .
But you might as well argue with a post a with Martha .
She go to town today -- I drive her to the station .
And you want to buy my platter .
Well , what will you give for it ? ''
`` Twenty dollar , '' say Anne , who be never mean to match business wit with a Copp , or she would not have offer her price at the start .
`` Well , I 'll see , '' say Miss Sarah cautiously .
`` That platter be mine fortunately , or I 'd never dare to sell it when Martha be n't here .
As it be , I daresay she 'll raise a fuss .
Martha 's the bos of this establishment I can tell you .
I 'm get awful tire of live under another woman 's thumb .
But come in , come in .
You must be real tired and hungry .
I 'll do the best I can for you in the way of tea but I warn you not to expect anything but bread and butter and some cowcumbers .
Martha lock up all the cake and cheese and preserve afore she go .
She always do , because she say I 'm too extravagant with them if company come . ''
The girl be hungry enough to do justice to any fare , and they enjoy Miss Sarah 's excellent bread and butter and `` cowcumbers '' thoroughly .
When the meal be over Miss Sarah say , `` I do n't know a I mind sell the platter .
But it 's worth twenty-five dollar .
It 's a very old platter . ''
Diana give Anne 's foot a gentle kick under the table , meaning , `` Do n't agree -- she 'll let it go for twenty if you hold out . ''
But Anne be not mind to take any chance in regard to that precious platter .
She promptly agree to give twenty-five and Miss Sarah look a if she felt sorry she have n't ask for thirty .
`` Well , I guess you may have it .
I want all the money I can scare up just now .
The fact be -- '' Miss Sarah throw up her head importantly , with a proud flush on her thin cheek -- `` I 'm go to be marry -- to Luther Wallace .
He want me twenty year ago .
I like him real well but he be poor then and father pack him off .
I s ` pose I should n't have let him go so meek but I be timid and frighten of father .
Besides , I do n't know men be so skurse . ''
When the girl be safely away , Diana driving and Anne hold the coveted platter carefully on her lap , the green , rain-freshened solitude of the Tory Road be enliven by ripple of girlish laughter .
`` I 'll amuse your Aunt Josephine with the ` strange eventful history ' of this afternoon when I go to town tomorrow .
We 've have a rather try time but it 's over now .
I 've get the platter , and that rain have lay the dust beautifully .
So ` all 's well that end well . ' ''
`` We 're not home yet , '' say Diana rather pessimistically , `` and there 's no tell what may happen before we be .
You 're such a girl to have adventure , Anne . ''
`` Having adventure come natural to some people , '' say Anne serenely .
`` You just have a gift for them or you have n't . ''
XIX Just a Happy Day `` After all , '' Anne have say to Marilla once , `` I believe the nice and sweet day be not those on which anything very splendid or wonderful or exciting happens but just those that bring simple little pleasure , follow one another softly , like pearl slip off a string . ''
Life at Green Gables be full of just such day , for Anne 's adventure and misadventure , like those of other people , do not all happen at once , but be sprinkle over the year , with long stretch of harmless , happy day between , fill with work and dream and laughter and lesson .
Such a day come late in August .
In the forenoon Anne and Diana row the delighted twin down the pond to the sandshore to pick `` sweet grass '' and paddle in the surf , over which the wind be harp an old lyric learn when the world be young .
In the afternoon Anne walk down to the old Irving place to see Paul .
She find him stretch out on the grassy bank beside the thick fir grove that shelter the house on the north , absorb in a book of fairy tale .
He spring up radiantly at sight of her .
`` Oh , I 'm so glad you 've come , teacher , '' he say eagerly , `` because Grandma 's away .
You 'll stay and have tea with me , wo n't you ?
It 's so lonesome to have tea all by oneself .
YOU know , teacher .
I 've have serious thought of ask Young Mary Joe to sit down and eat her tea with me , but I expect Grandma would n't approve .
She say the French have to be keep in their place .
And anyhow , it 's difficult to talk with Young Mary Joe .
She just laugh and say , ` Well , yous do beat all de kid I ever know . '
That be n't my idea of conversation . ''
`` Of course I 'll stay to tea , '' say Anne gaily .
`` I be die to be ask .
My mouth have be water for some more of your grandma 's delicious shortbread ever since I have tea here before . ''
Paul look very sober .
`` If it depend on me , teacher , '' he say , stand before Anne with his hand in his pocket and his beautiful little face shadow with sudden care , `` You should have shortbread with a right good will .
But it depend on Mary Joe .
I hear Grandma tell her before she leave that she be n't to give me any shortcake because it be too rich for little boy ' stomach .
But maybe Mary Joe will cut some for you if I promise I wo n't eat any .
Let u hope for the best . ''
`` Yes , let u , '' agree Anne , whom this cheerful philosophy suit exactly , `` and if Mary Joe prove hard-hearted and wo n't give me any shortbread it do n't matter in the least , so you be not to worry over that . ''
`` You 're sure you wo n't mind if she do n't ? ''
say Paul anxiously .
`` Perfectly sure , dear heart . ''
`` Then I wo n't worry , '' say Paul , with a long breath of relief , `` especially a I really think Mary Joe will listen to reason .
She 's not a naturally unreasonable person , but she have learn by experience that it do n't do to disobey Grandma 's order .
Grandma be an excellent woman but people must do a she tell them .
She be very much please with me this morning because I manage at last to eat all my plateful of porridge .
It be a great effort but I succeed .
Grandma say she think she 'll make a man of me yet .
But , teacher , I want to ask you a very important question .
You will answer it truthfully , wo n't you ? ''
`` I 'll try , '' promise Anne .
`` Do you think I 'm wrong in my upper story ? ''
ask Paul , a if his very existence depend on her reply .
`` Goodness , no , Paul , '' exclaim Anne in amazement .
`` Certainly you 're not .
What put such an idea into your head ? ''
`` Mary Joe ... but she do n't know I hear her .
Mrs. Peter Sloane 's hired girl , Veronica , come to see Mary Joe last evening and I hear them talk in the kitchen a I be go through the hall .
I hear Mary Joe say , ` Dat Paul , he be de queeres ' leetle boy .
He talk dat queer .
I tink dere 's someting wrong in his upper story . '
I could n't sleep last night for ever so long , thinking of it , and wonder if Mary Joe be right .
I could n't bear to ask Grandma about it somehow , but I make up my mind I 'd ask you .
I 'm so glad you think I 'm all right in my upper story . ''
`` Of course you be .
Mary Joe be a silly , ignorant girl , and you be never to worry about anything she say , '' say Anne indignantly , secretly resolve to give Mrs. Irving a discreet hint a to the advisability of restrain Mary Joe 's tongue .
`` Well , that 's a weight off my mind , '' say Paul .
`` I 'm perfectly happy now , teacher , thanks to you .
It would n't be nice to have something wrong in your upper story , would it , teacher ?
I suppose the reason Mary Joe imago I have be because I tell her what I think about thing sometimes . ''
`` It be a rather dangerous practice , '' admit Anne , out of the depth of her own experience .
`` Well , by and by I 'll tell you the thought I tell Mary Joe and you can see for yourself if there 's anything queer in them , '' say Paul , `` but I 'll wait till it begin to get dark .
That be the time I ache to tell people thing , and when nobody else be handy I just HAVE to tell Mary Joe .
But after this I wo n't , if it make her imagine I 'm wrong in my upper story .
I 'll just ache and bear it . ''
`` And if the ache get too bad you can come up to Green Gables and tell me your thought , '' suggest Anne , with all the gravity that endear her to child , who so dearly love to be take seriously .
`` Yes , I will .
But I hope Davy wo n't be there when I go because he make face at me .
I do n't mind VERY much because he be such a little boy and I be quite a big one , but still it be not pleasant to have face make at you .
And Davy make such terrible one .
Sometimes I be frighten he will never get his face straighten out again .
He make them at me in church when I ought to be think of sacred thing .
Dora like me though , and I like her , but not so well a I do before she tell Minnie May Barry that she mean to marry me when I grow up .
I may marry somebody when I grow up but I 'm far too young to be think of it yet , do n't you think , teacher ? ''
`` Rather young , '' agree teacher .
`` Speaking of marrying , remind me of another thing that have be trouble me of late , '' continue Paul .
`` Mrs. Lynde be down here one day last week have tea with Grandma , and Grandma make me show her my little mother 's picture ... the one father send me for my birthday present .
I do n't exactly want to show it to Mrs. Lynde .
Mrs. Lynde be a good , kind woman , but she be n't the sort of person you want to show your mother 's picture to .
YOU know , teacher .
But of course I obey Grandma .
Mrs. Lynde say she be very pretty but kind of actressy look , and must have be an awful lot young than father .
Then she say , ` Some of these day your pa will be marry again likely .
How will you like to have a new ma , Master Paul ? '
Well , the idea almost take my breath away , teacher , but I be n't go to let Mrs. Lynde see THAT .
I just look her straight in the face ... like this ... and I say , ` Mrs. Lynde , father make a pretty good job of pick out my first mother and I could trust him to pick out just as good a one the second time . '
And I CAN trust him , teacher .
But still , I hope , if he ever do give me a new mother , he 'll ask my opinion about her before it 's too late .
There 's Mary Joe come to call u to tea .
I 'll go and consult with her about the shortbread . ''
As a result of the `` consultation , '' Mary Joe cut the shortbread and add a dish of preserve to the bill of fare .
Anne pour the tea and she and Paul have a very merry meal in the dim old sitting room whose window be open to the gulf breeze , and they talk so much `` nonsense '' that Mary Joe be quite scandalize and tell Veronica the next evening that `` de school mees '' be as queer a Paul .
After tea Paul take Anne up to his room to show her his mother 's picture , which have be the mysterious birthday present keep by Mrs. Irving in the bookcase .
Paul 's little low-ceilinged room be a soft whirl of ruddy light from the sun that be set over the sea and swing shadow from the fir tree that grow close to the square , deep-set window .
From out this soft glow and glamor shone a sweet , girlish face , with tender mother eye , that be hang on the wall at the foot of the bed .
`` That 's my little mother , '' say Paul with love pride .
`` I get Grandma to hang it there where I 'd see it as soon a I open my eye in the morning .
I never mind not have the light when I go to bed now , because it just seem a if my little mother be right here with me .
Father know just what I would like for a birthday present , although he never ask me .
Is n't it wonderful how much father DO know ? ''
`` Your mother be very lovely , Paul , and you look a little like her .
But her eye and hair be darker than yours . ''
`` My eye be the same color a father 's , '' say Paul , fly about the room to heap all available cushion on the window seat , `` but father 's hair be gray .
He have lot of it , but it be gray .
You see , father be nearly fifty .
That 's ripe old age , be n't it ?
But it 's only OUTSIDE he 's old .
INSIDE he 's just as young a anybody .
Now , teacher , please sit here ; and I 'll sit at your foot .
May I lay my head against your knee ?
That 's the way my little mother and I use to sit .
Oh , this be real splendid , I think . ''
`` Now , I want to hear those thought which Mary Joe pronounces so queer , '' say Anne , pat the mop of curl at her side .
Paul never need any coax to tell his thought ... at least , to congenial soul .
`` I think them out in the fir grove one night , '' he say dreamily .
`` Of course I do n't BELIEVE them but I THOUGHT them .
YOU know , teacher .
And then I want to tell them to somebody and there be nobody but Mary Joe .
Mary Joe be in the pantry set bread and I sit down on the bench beside her and I say , ` Mary Joe , do you know what I think ?
I think the evening star be a lighthouse on the land where the fairy dwell . '
And Mary Joe say , ` Well , yous be de queer one .
Dare ai n't no such ting a fairy . '
I be very much provoke .
Of course , I know there be no fairy ; but that need n't prevent my thinking there be .
You know , teacher .
But I try again quite patiently .
I say , ` Well then , Mary Joe , do you know what I think ?
I think an angel walk over the world after the sun set ... a great , tall , white angel , with silvery fold wing ... and sing the flower and bird to sleep .
Children can hear him if they know how to listen . '
Then Mary Joe hold up her hand all over flour and say , ` Well , yous be de queer leetle boy .
Yous make me feel scare . '
And she really do look scar .
I go out then and whisper the rest of my thought to the garden .
There be a little birch tree in the garden and it die .
Grandma say the salt spray kill it ; but I think the dryad belonging to it be a foolish dryad who wander away to see the world and get lose .
And the little tree be so lonely it die of a broken heart . ''
`` And when the poor , foolish little dryad get tire of the world and come back to her tree HER heart will break , '' say Anne .
`` Yes ; but if dryad be foolish they must take the consequence , just a if they be real people , '' say Paul gravely .
`` Do you know what I think about the new moon , teacher ?
I think it be a little golden boat full of dream . ''
`` And when it tip on a cloud some of them spill out and fall into your sleep . ''
`` Exactly , teacher .
Oh , you DO know .
And I think the violet be little snip of the sky that fell down when the angel cut out hole for the star to shine through .
And the buttercup be make out of old sunshine ; and I think the sweet pea will be butterfly when they go to heaven .
Now , teacher , do you see anything so very queer about those thought ? ''
`` No , laddie dear , they be not queer at all ; they be strange and beautiful thought for a little boy to think , and so people who could n't think anything of the sort themselves , if they try for a hundred year , think them queer .
But keep on think them , Paul ... some day you be go to be a poet , I believe . ''
When Anne reach home she find a very different type of boyhood wait to be put to bed .
Davy be sulky ; and when Anne have undress him he bounce into bed and bury his face in the pillow .
`` Davy , you have forget to say your prayer , '' say Anne rebukingly .
`` No , I do n't forget , '' say Davy defiantly , `` but I ai n't go to say my prayer any more .
I 'm go to give up try to be good , 'cause no matter how good I be you 'd like Paul Irving better .
So I might as well be bad and have the fun of it . ''
`` I do n't like Paul Irving BETTER , '' say Anne seriously .
`` I like you just as well , only in a different way . ''
`` But I want you to like me the same way , '' pout Davy .
`` You ca n't like different people the same way .
You do n't like Dora and me the same way , do you ? ''
Davy sit up and reflect .
`` No ... o ... o , '' he admit at last , `` I like Dora because she 's my sister but I like you because you 're YOU . ''
`` And I like Paul because he be Paul and Davy because he be Davy , '' say Anne gaily .
`` Well , I kind of wish I 'd say my prayer then , '' say Davy , convince by this logic .
`` But it 's too much bother get out now to say them .
I 'll say them twice over in the morning , Anne .
Wo n't that do as well ? ''
No , Anne be positive it would not do as well .
So Davy scramble out and kneel down at her knee .
When he have finish his devotion he lean back on his little , bare , brown heel and look up at her .

In the twilight Anne saunter down to the Dryad 's Bubble and saw Gilbert Blythe come down through the dusky Haunted Wood .

''

but so few people ever come here because it be so far out of the way .

XXII Odds and Ends `` So you have tea at the stone house with Lavendar Lewis ? ''

XXIII Miss Lavendar 's Romance `` I think I 'll take a walk through to Echo Lodge this evening , '' say Anne , one Friday afternoon in December .
`` It look like snow , '' say Marilla dubiously .
`` I 'll be there before the snow come and I mean to stay all night .
Diana ca n't go because she have company , and I 'm sure Miss Lavendar will be look for me tonight .
It 's a whole fortnight since I be there . ''
Anne have pay many a visit to Echo Lodge since that October day .
Sometimes she and Diana drive around by the road ; sometimes they walk through the wood .
When Diana could not go Anne go alone .
Between her and Miss Lavendar have spring up one of those fervent , helpful friendship possible only between a woman who have keep the freshness of youth in her heart and soul , and a girl whose imagination and intuition supply the place of experience .
Anne have at last discover a real `` kindred spirit , '' while into the little lady 's lonely , sequester life of dream Anne and Diana come with the wholesome joy and exhilaration of the outer existence , which Miss Lavendar , `` the world forgetting , by the world forgot , '' have long cease to share ; they bring an atmosphere of youth and reality to the little stone house .
Charlotta the Fourth always greet them with her very wide smile ... and Charlotta 's smile WERE fearfully wide ... love them for the sake of her adored mistress as well a for their own .
Never have there be such `` high jinks '' hold in the little stone house a be hold there that beautiful , late-lingering autumn , when November seem October over again , and even December ape the sunshine and haze of summer .
But on this particular day it seem a if December have remember that it be time for winter and have turn suddenly dull and brooding , with a windless hush predictive of come snow .
Nevertheless , Anne keenly enjoy her walk through the great gray maze of the beechlands ; though alone she never find it lonely ; her imagination people her path with merry companion , and with these she carry on a gay , pretend conversation that be witty and more fascinating than conversation be apt to be in real life , where people sometimes fail most lamentably to talk up to the requirement .
In a `` make believe '' assembly of choice spirit everybody say just the thing you want her to say and so give you the chance to say just what YOU want to say .
Attended by this invisible company , Anne traverse the wood and arrive at the fir lane just a broad , feathery flake begin to flutter down softly .

And I ca n't reconcile myself to it a most woman seem to .
I 'm just as rebellious a I be when I discover my first gray hair .

Seventeen CA N'T understand .

You always bring youth in your hand like a gift .
We 're go to have a jolly evening .
Tea first ... what do you want for tea ?
We 'll have whatever you like .
Do think of something nice and indigestible . ''
There be sound of riot and mirth in the little stone house that night .
What with cooking and feasting and make candy and laughing and `` pretending , '' it be quite true that Miss Lavendar and Anne comport themselves in a fashion entirely unsuited to the dignity of a spinster of forty-five and a sedate schoolma'am .
Then , when they be tire , they sit down on the rug before the grate in the parlor , light only by the soft fireshine and perfume deliciously by Miss Lavendar 's open rose-jar on the mantel .

`` If you be n't I should be blue ... very blue ... almost navy blue .
Dreams and make-believe be all very well in the daytime and the sunshine , but when dark and storm come they fail to satisfy .
One want real thing then .
But you do n't know this ... seventeen never know it .
At seventeen dream DO satisfy because you think the reality be wait for you further on .

`` I always like to do thing as well a possible , '' say Miss Lavendar meditatively , `` and since an old maid I have to be I be determine to be a very nice one .
People say I 'm odd ; but it 's just because I follow my own way of be an old maid and refuse to copy the traditional pattern .

He have a rival or two , you see .

I know I would rather be an old maid for a thousand year than marry anybody who be n't Stephen Irving .

Is n't this candy scrumptious ?
I 've eat far more than be good for me already but I 'm go to keep recklessly on . ''

she whisper .

Boys do n't .
You know , Miss Lewis .

XXIV A Prophet in His Own Country One May day Avonlea folk be mildly excite over some `` Avonlea Notes , '' sign `` Observer , '' which appear in the Charlottetown ` Daily Enterprise . '
Gossip ascribe the authorship thereof to Charlie Sloane , partly because the say Charlie have indulge in similar literary flight in time past , and partly because one of the note seem to embody a sneer at Gilbert Blythe .
Avonlea juvenile society persist in regard Gilbert Blythe and Charlie Sloane a rival in the good grace of a certain damsel with gray eye and an imagination .
Gossip , a usual , be wrong .

Only two of the note have any bearing on this history : `` Rumor have it that there will be a wedding in our village ere the daisy be in bloom .
A new and highly respect citizen will lead to the hymeneal altar one of our most popular lady .
`` Uncle Abe , our well-known weather prophet , predict a violent storm of thunder and lightning for the evening of the twenty-third of May , begin at seven o'clock sharp .

Life in Avonlea continue on the smooth and even tenor of it way .

XXV An Avonlea Scandal One blithe June morning , a fortnight after Uncle Abe 's storm , Anne come slowly through the Green Gables yard from the garden , carry in her hand two blight stalk of white narcissus .

Anne Shirley !
