`` Milty have a cat that take fit ... she 's take a fit regular every day for three week .
Milty say it 's awful fun to watch her .
I go down today on purpose to see her have one but the mean old thing would n't take a fit and just keep healthy a healthy , though Milty and me hung round all the afternoon and wait .
But never mind '' ... Davy brighten up a the insidious comfort of the plum jam stole into his soul ... `` maybe I 'll see her in one sometime yet .
It do n't seem likely she 'd stop have them all at once when she 's be so in the habit of it , do it ?
This jam be awful nice . ''
Davy have no sorrow that plum jam could not cure .
Sunday prove so rainy that there be no stir abroad ; but by Monday everybody have hear some version of the Harrison story .
The school buzz with it and Davy come home , full of information .
`` Marilla , Mr. Harrison have a new wife ... well , not ezackly new , but they 've stop be marry for quite a spell , Milty say .
I always s ` posed people have to keep on be marry once they 'd begin , but Milty say no , there 's way of stop if you ca n't agree .
Milty say one way be just to start off and leave your wife , and that 's what Mr. Harrison do .
Milty say Mr. Harrison leave his wife because she throw thing at him ... HARD thing ... and Arty Sloane say it be because she would n't let him smoke , and Ned Clay say it be 'cause she never let up scold him .
I would n't leave MY wife for anything like that .
I 'd just put my foot down and say , ` Mrs. Davy , you 've just get to do what 'll please ME 'cause I 'm a MAN . '
THAT 'D settle her pretty quick I guess .
But Annetta Clay say SHE leave HIM because he would n't scrape his boot at the door and she do n't blame her .
I 'm go right over to Mr. Harrison 's this minute to see what she 's like . ''
Davy soon return , somewhat cast down .
`` Mrs. Harrison be away ... she 's go to Carmody with Mrs. Rachel Lynde to get new paper for the parlor .
And Mr. Harrison say to tell Anne to go over and see him 'cause he want to have a talk with her .
And say , the floor be scrub , and Mr. Harrison be shave , though there be n't any preaching yesterday . ''
The Harrison kitchen wear a very unfamiliar look to Anne .
The floor be indeed scrub to a wonderful pitch of purity and so be every article of furniture in the room ; the stove be polish until she could see her face in it ; the wall be whitewash and the window pane sparkle in the sunlight .
By the table sit Mr. Harrison in his working clothes , which on Friday have be note for sundry rent and tatter but which be now neatly patch and brush .
He be sprucely shave and what little hair he have be carefully trim .
`` Sit down , Anne , sit down , '' say Mr. Harrison in a tone but two degree remove from that which Avonlea people use at funeral .
`` Emily 's go over to Carmody with Rachel Lynde ... she 's strike up a lifelong friendship already with Rachel Lynde .
Beats all how contrary woman be .
Well , Anne , my easy time be over ... all over .
It 's neatness and tidiness for me for the rest of my natural life , I suppose . ''
Mr. Harrison do his best to speak dolefully , but an irrepressible twinkle in his eye betray him .
`` Mr. Harrison , you be glad your wife be come back , '' cry Anne , shake her finger at him .
`` You need n't pretend you 're not , because I can see it plainly . ''
Mr. Harrison relax into a sheepish smile .
`` Well ... well ... I 'm get use to it , '' he concede .
`` I ca n't say I be sorry to see Emily .
A man really need some protection in a community like this , where he ca n't play a game of checker with a neighbor without be accuse of want to marry that neighbor 's sister and have it put in the paper . ''
`` Nobody would have suppose you go to see Isabella Andrews if you have n't pretend to be unmarried , '' say Anne severely .
`` I do n't pretend I be .
If anybody 'd have ask me if I be marry I 'd have say I be .
But they just take it for grant .
I be n't anxious to talk about the matter ... I be feel too sore over it .
It would have be nut for Mrs. Rachel Lynde if she have know my wife have leave me , would n't it now ? ''
`` But some people say that you leave her . ''
`` She start it , Anne , she start it .
I 'm go to tell you the whole story , for I do n't want you to think bad of me than I deserve ... nor of Emily neither .
But let 's go out on the veranda .
Everything be so fearful neat in here that it kind of make me homesick .
I suppose I 'll get use to it after awhile but it ease me up to look at the yard .
Emily have n't have time to tidy it up yet . ''
As soon a they be comfortably seat on the veranda Mr. Harrison begin his tale of woe .
`` I live in Scottsford , New Brunswick , before I come here , Anne .
My sister keep house for me and she suit me fine ; she be just reasonably tidy and she let me alone and spoil me ... so Emily say .
But three year ago she die .
Before she die she worry a lot about what be to become of me and finally she get me to promise I 'd get marry .
She advise me to take Emily Scott because Emily have money of her own and be a pattern housekeeper .
I say , say I , 'Em ily Scott would n't look at me . '
` You ask her and see , ' say my sister ; and just to ease her mind I promise her I would ... and I do .
And Emily say she 'd have me .
Never be so surprised in my life , Anne ... a smart pretty little woman like her and an old fellow like me .
I tell you I think at first I be in luck .
Well , we be marry and take a little wedding trip to St. John for a fortnight and then we go home .
We get home at ten o'clock at night , and I give you my word , Anne , that in half an hour that woman be at work housecleaning .
Oh , I know you 're think my house need it ... you 've get a very expressive face , Anne ; your thought just come out on it like print ... but it do n't , not that bad .
It have get pretty mixed up while I be keep bachelor 's hall , I admit , but I 'd get a woman to come in and clean it up before I be marry and there 'd be considerable painting and fix do .
I tell you if you take Emily into a brand new white marble palace she 'd be into the scrubbing as soon a she could get an old dress on .
Well , she clean house till one o'clock that night and at four she be up and at it again .
And she keep on that way ... far 's I could see she never stop .
It be scour and sweep and dust everlasting , except on Sundays , and then she be just long for Monday to begin again .
But it be her way of amuse herself and I could have reconcile myself to it if she 'd leave me alone .
But that she would n't do .
She 'd set out to make me over but she have n't catch me young enough .
I be n't allow to come into the house unless I change my boot for slipper at the door .
I dars n't smoke a pipe for my life unless I go to the barn .
And I do n't use good enough grammar .
Emily 'd be a schoolteacher in her early life and she 'd never get over it .
Then she hat to see me eat with my knife .
Well , there it be , pick and nag everlasting .
But I s ` pose , Anne , to be fair , I be cantankerous too .
I do n't try to improve a I might have do ... I just get cranky and disagreeable when she find fault .
I tell her one day she have n't complain of my grammar when I propose to her .
It be n't an overly tactful thing to say .
A woman would forgive a man for beat her sooner than for hint she be too much please to get him .
Well , we bicker along like that and it be n't exactly pleasant , but we might have get use to each other after a spell if it have n't be for Ginger .
Ginger be the rock we split on at last .
Emily do n't like parrot and she could n't stand Ginger 's profane habit of speech .
I be attach to the bird for my brother the sailor 's sake .
My brother the sailor be a pet of mine when we be little tad and he 'd send Ginger to me when he be die .
I do n't see any sense in get work up over his swearing .
There 's nothing I hate bad 'n profanity in a human be , but in a parrot , that 's just repeat what it 's hear with no more understanding of it than I 'd have of Chinese , allowance might be make .
But Emily could n't see it that way .
Women ai n't logical .
She try to break Ginger of swear but she have n't any good success than she have in try to make me stop say ' I see ' and ` them thing . '
Seemed a if the more she try the bad Ginger get , same a me .
`` Well , thing go on like this , both of u get raspier , till the CLIMAX come .
Emily invite our minister and his wife to tea , and another minister and HIS wife that be visit them .
I 'd promise to put Ginger away in some safe place where nobody would hear him ... Emily would n't touch his cage with a ten-foot pole ... and I mean to do it , for I do n't want the minister to hear anything unpleasant in my house .
But it slip my mind ... Emily be worry me so much about clean collar and grammar that it be n't any wonder ... and I never think of that poor parrot till we sit down to tea .
Just a minister number one be in the very middle of say grace , Ginger , who be on the veranda outside the dining room window , lift up HIS voice .
The gobbler have come into view in the yard and the sight of a gobbler always have an unwholesome effect on Ginger .
He surpass himself that time .
You can smile , Anne , and I do n't deny I 've chuckle some over it since myself , but at the time I felt almost a much mortify a Emily .
I go out and carry Ginger to the barn .
I ca n't say I enjoy the meal .
I know by the look of Emily that there be trouble brewing for Ginger and James A .
When the folk go away I start for the cow pasture and on the way I do some thinking .
I felt sorry for Emily and kind of fancied I have n't be so thoughtful of her a I might ; and besides , I wonder if the minister would think that Ginger have learn his vocabulary from me .
The long and short of it be , I decide that Ginger would have to be mercifully dispose of and when I 'd druv the cow home I go in to tell Emily so .
But there be no Emily and there be a letter on the table ... just accord to the rule in story book .
Emily writ that I 'd have to choose between her and Ginger ; she 'd go back to her own house and there she would stay till I go and tell her I 'd get rid of that parrot .
`` I be all rile up , Anne , and I say she might stay till doomsday if she wait for that ; and I stick to it .
I pack up her belonging and send them after her .
It make an awful lot of talk ... Scottsford be pretty near as bad a Avonlea for gossip ... and everybody sympathize with Emily .
It keep me all cross and cantankerous and I saw I 'd have to get out or I 'd never have any peace .
I conclude I 'd come to the Island .
I 'd be here when I be a boy and I like it ; but Emily have always say she would n't live in a place where folk be scar to walk out after dark for fear they 'd fall off the edge .
So , just to be contrary , I move over here .
And that 's all there be to it .
I have n't ever hear a word from or about Emily till I come home from the back field Saturday and find her scrub the floor but with the first decent dinner I 'd have since she leave me all ready on the table .
She tell me to eat it first and then we 'd talk ... by which I conclude that Emily have learn some lesson about get along with a man .
So she 's here and she 's go to stay ... see that Ginger 's dead and the Island 's some big than she think .
There 's Mrs. Lynde and her now .
No , do n't go , Anne .
Stay and get acquaint with Emily .
She take quite a notion to you Saturday ... want to know who that handsome redhaired girl be at the next house . ''
Mrs. Harrison welcome Anne radiantly and insist on her stay to tea .
`` James A. have be tell me all about you and how kind you 've be , make cake and thing for him , '' she say .
`` I want to get acquaint with all my new neighbor just as soon a possible .
Mrs. Lynde be a lovely woman , be n't she ?
So friendly . ''
When Anne go home in the sweet June dusk , Mrs. Harrison go with her across the field where the firefly be light their starry lamp .
`` I suppose , '' say Mrs. Harrison confidentially , `` that James A. have tell you our story ? ''
`` Yes . ''
`` Then I need n't tell it , for James A. be a just man and he would tell the truth .
The blame be far from be all on his side .
I can see that now .
I be n't back in my own house an hour before I wish I have n't be so hasty but I would n't give in .
I see now that I expect too much of a man .
And I be real foolish to mind his bad grammar .
It do n't matter if a man do use bad grammar so long a he be a good provider and do n't go poke round the pantry to see how much sugar you 've use in a week .
I feel that James A. and I be go to be real happy now .
I wish I know who ` Observer ' be , so that I could thank him .
I owe him a real debt of gratitude . ''
Anne keep her own counsel and Mrs. Harrison never know that her gratitude find it way to it object .
Anne felt rather bewilder over the far-reaching consequence of those foolish `` note . ''
They have reconcile a man to his wife and make the reputation of a prophet .
Mrs. Lynde be in the Green Gables kitchen .
She have be tell the whole story to Marilla .
`` Well , and how do you like Mrs. Harrison ? ''
she ask Anne .
`` Very much .
I think she 's a real nice little woman . ''
`` That 's exactly what she be , '' say Mrs. Rachel with emphasis , `` and a I 've just be sayin ' to Marilla , I think we ought all to overlook Mr. Harrison 's peculiarity for her sake and try to make her feel at home here , that 's what .
Well , I must get back .
Thomas 'll be weary for me .
I get out a little since Eliza come and he 's seem a lot good these past few day , but I never like to be long away from him .
I hear Gilbert Blythe have resign from White Sands .
He 'll be off to college in the fall , I suppose . ''
Mrs. Rachel look sharply at Anne , but Anne be bend over a sleepy Davy nodding on the sofa and nothing be to be read in her face .
She carry Davy away , her oval girlish cheek press against his curly yellow head .
As they go up the stair Davy fling a tired arm about Anne 's neck and give her a warm hug and a sticky kiss .
`` You 're awful nice , Anne .
Milty Boulter write on his slate today and show it to Jennie Sloane , '' ` Roses red and vi ` let blue , Sugar 's sweet , and so be you '' and that ` spresses my feeling for you ezackly , Anne . ''
XXVI Around the Bend Thomas Lynde fade out of life a quietly and unobtrusively a he have live it .
His wife be a tender , patient , unwearied nurse .
Sometimes Rachel have be a little hard on her Thomas in health , when his slowness or meekness have provoke her ; but when he become ill no voice could be low , no hand more gently skillful , no vigil more uncomplaining .
`` You 've be a good wife to me , Rachel , '' he once say simply , when she be sit by him in the dusk , hold his thin , blanch old hand in her work-hardened one .
`` A good wife .
I 'm sorry I ai n't leave you good off ; but the child will look after you .
They 're all smart , capable child , just like their mother .
A good mother ... a good woman ... . ''
He have fall asleep then , and the next morning , just a the white dawn be creep up over the pointed fir in the hollow , Marilla go softly into the east gable and waken Anne .
`` Anne , Thomas Lynde be go ... their hired boy just bring the word .
I 'm go right down to Rachel . ''
On the day after Thomas Lynde 's funeral Marilla go about Green Gables with a strangely preoccupied air .
Occasionally she look at Anne , seem on the point of say something , then shake her head and button up her mouth .
After tea she go down to see Mrs. Rachel ; and when she return she go to the east gable , where Anne be correct school exercise .
`` How be Mrs. Lynde tonight ? ''
ask the latter .
`` She 's feel calm and more composed , '' answer Marilla , sit down on Anne 's bed ... a proceeding which betoken some unusual mental excitement , for in Marilla 's code of household ethic to sit on a bed after it be make up be an unpardonable offense .
`` But she 's very lonely .
Eliza have to go home today ... her son be n't well and she felt she could n't stay any long . ''
`` When I 've finish these exercise I 'll run down and chat awhile with Mrs. Lynde , '' say Anne .
`` I have intend to study some Latin composition tonight but it can wait . ''
`` I suppose Gilbert Blythe be go to college in the fall , '' say Marilla jerkily .
`` How would you like to go too , Anne ? ''
Anne look up in astonishment .
`` I would like it , of course , Marilla .
But it be n't possible . ''
`` I guess it can be make possible .
I 've always felt that you should go .
I 've never felt easy to think you be give it all up on my account . ''
`` But Marilla , I 've never be sorry for a moment that I stay home .
I 've be so happy ... Oh , these past two year have just be delightful . ''
`` Oh , yes , I know you 've be content enough .
But that be n't the question exactly .
You ought to go on with your education .
You 've save enough to put you through one year at Redmond and the money the stock bring in will do for another year ... and there 's scholarship and thing you might win . ''
`` Yes , but I ca n't go , Marilla .
Your eye be well , of course ; but I ca n't leave you alone with the twin .
They need so much look after . ''
`` I wo n't be alone with them .
That 's what I mean to discuss with you .
I have a long talk with Rachel tonight .
Anne , she 's feel dreadful bad over a good many thing .
She 's not left very well off .
It seem they mortgage the farm eight year ago to give the young boy a start when he go west ; and they 've never be able to pay much more than the interest since .
And then of course Thomas ' illness have cost a good deal , one way or another .
The farm will have to be sell and Rachel think there 'll be hardly anything leave after the bill be settle .
She say she 'll have to go and live with Eliza and it 's break her heart to think of leave Avonlea .
A woman of her age do n't make new friend and interest easy .
And , Anne , a she talk about it the thought come to me that I would ask her to come and live with me , but I think I ought to talk it over with you first before I say anything to her .
If I have Rachel live with me you could go to college .
How do you feel about it ? ''
`` I feel ... a if ... somebody ... have hand me ... the moon ... and I do n't know ... exactly ... what to do ... with it , '' say Anne dazedly .
`` But a for ask Mrs. Lynde to come here , that be for you to decide , Marilla .
Do you think ... be you sure ... you would like it ?
Mrs. Lynde be a good woman and a kind neighbor , but ... but ... '' `` But she 's get her fault , you mean to say ?
Well , she have , of course ; but I think I 'd rather put up with far bad fault than see Rachel go away from Avonlea .
I 'd miss her terrible .
She 's the only close friend I 've get here and I 'd be lose without her .
We 've be neighbor for forty-five year and we 've never have a quarrel ... though we come rather near it that time you fly at Mrs. Rachel for call you homely and redhaired .
Do you remember , Anne ? ''
`` I should think I do , '' say Anne ruefully .
`` People do n't forget thing like that .
How I hat poor Mrs. Rachel at that moment ! ''
`` And then that ` apology ' you make her .
Well , you be a handful , in all conscience , Anne .
I do feel so puzzled and bewilder how to manage you .
Matthew understood you good . ''
`` Matthew understood everything , '' say Anne softly , a she always speak of him .
`` Well , I think it could be manage so that Rachel and I would n't clash at all .
It always seem to me that the reason two woman ca n't get along in one house be that they try to share the same kitchen and get in each other 's way .
Now , if Rachel come here , she could have the north gable for her bedroom and the spare room for a kitchen as well a not , for we do n't really need a spare room at all .
She could put her stove there and what furniture she want to keep , and be real comfortable and independent .
She 'll have enough to live on of course ... her child 'll see to that ... so all I 'd be give her would be house room .
Yes , Anne , far a I 'm concerned I 'd like it . ''
`` Then ask her , '' say Anne promptly .
`` I 'd be very sorry myself to see Mrs. Rachel go away . ''
`` And if she come , '' continue Marilla , `` You can go to college as well a not .
She 'll be company for me and she 'll do for the twin what I ca n't do , so there 's no reason in the world why you should n't go . ''
Anne have a long meditation at her window that night .
Joy and regret struggle together in her heart .
She have come at last ... suddenly and unexpectedly ... to the bend in the road ; and college be around it , with a hundred rainbow hope and vision ; but Anne realize as well that when she round that curve she must leave many sweet thing behind ... all the little simple duty and interest which have grow so dear to her in the last two year and which she have glorify into beauty and delight by the enthusiasm she have put into them .
She must give up her school ... and she love every one of her pupil , even the stupid and naughty one .
The mere thought of Paul Irving make her wonder if Redmond be such a name to conjure with after all .
`` I 've put out a lot of little root these two year , '' Anne tell the moon , `` and when I 'm pull up they 're go to hurt a great deal .
But it 's best to go , I think , and , a Marilla say , there 's no good reason why I should n't .
I must get out all my ambition and dust them . ''
Anne send in her resignation the next day ; and Mrs. Rachel , after a heart to heart talk with Marilla , gratefully accept the offer of a home at Green Gables .
She elect to remain in her own house for the summer , however ; the farm be not to be sell until the fall and there be many arrangement to be make .
`` I certainly never think of living a far off the road a Green Gables , '' sigh Mrs. Rachel to herself .
`` But really , Green Gables do n't seem a out of the world a it use to do ... Anne have lot of company and the twin make it real lively .
And anyhow , I 'd rather live at the bottom of a well than leave Avonlea . ''
These two decision be noise abroad speedily oust the arrival of Mrs. Harrison in popular gossip .
Sage head be shake over Marilla Cuthbert 's rash step in ask Mrs. Rachel to live with her .
People opine that they would n't get on together .
They be both `` too fond of their own way , '' and many doleful prediction be make , none of which disturb the party in question at all .
They have come to a clear and distinct understanding of the respective duty and right of their new arrangement and meant to abide by them .
`` I wo n't meddle with you nor you with me , '' Mrs. Rachel have say decidedly , `` and a for the twin , I 'll be glad to do all I can for them ; but I wo n't undertake to answer Davy 's question , that 's what .
I 'm not an encyclopedia , neither be I a Philadelphia lawyer .
You 'll miss Anne for that . ''
`` Sometimes Anne 's answer be about as queer a Davy 's question , '' say Marilla drily .
`` The twin will miss her and no mistake ; but her future ca n't be sacrifice to Davy 's thirst for information .
When he ask question I ca n't answer I 'll just tell him child should be see and not hear .
That be how I be bring up , and I do n't know but what it be just as good a way a all these new-fangled notion for train child . ''
`` Well , Anne 's method seem to have work fairly well with Davy , '' say Mrs. Lynde smilingly .
`` He be a reformed character , that 's what . ''
`` He be n't a bad little soul , '' concede Marilla .
`` I never expect to get a fond of those child a I have .
Davy get round you somehow ... and Dora be a lovely child , although she be ... kind of ... well , kind of ... '' `` Monotonous ?
Exactly , '' supply Mrs. Rachel .
`` Like a book where every page be the same , that 's what .
Dora will make a good , reliable woman but she 'll never set the pond on fire .
Well , that sort of folk be comfortable to have round , even if they 're not as interesting a the other kind . ''
Gilbert Blythe be probably the only person to whom the news of Anne 's resignation bring unmixed pleasure .
Her pupil look upon it a a sheer catastrophe .
Annetta Bell have hysteric when she go home .
Anthony Pye fight two pitched and unprovoked battle with other boy by way of relieve his feeling .
Barbara Shaw cry all night .
Paul Irving defiantly tell his grandmother that she need n't expect him to eat any porridge for a week .
`` I ca n't do it , Grandma , '' he say .
`` I do n't really know if I can eat ANYTHING .
I feel a if there be a dreadful lump in my throat .
I 'd have cry come home from school if Jake Donnell have n't be watch me .
I believe I will cry after I go to bed .
It would n't show on my eye tomorrow , would it ?
And it would be such a relief .
But anyway , I ca n't eat porridge .
I 'm go to need all my strength of mind to bear up against this , Grandma , and I wo n't have any leave to grapple with porridge .
Oh Grandma , I do n't know what I 'll do when my beautiful teacher go away .
Milty Boulter say he bet Jane Andrews will get the school .
I suppose Miss Andrews be very nice .
But I know she wo n't understand thing like Miss Shirley . ''
Diana also take a very pessimistic view of affair .
`` It will be horribly lonesome here next winter , '' she mourn , one twilight when the moonlight be rain `` airy silver '' through the cherry bough and fill the east gable with a soft , dream-like radiance in which the two girl sit and talk , Anne on her low rocker by the window , Diana sit Turkfashion on the bed .
`` You and Gilbert will be go ... and the Allans too .
They be go to call Mr. Allan to Charlottetown and of course he 'll accept .
It 's too mean .
We 'll be vacant all winter , I suppose , and have to listen to a long string of candidate ... and half of them wo n't be any good . ''
`` I hope they wo n't call Mr. Baxter from East Grafton here , anyhow , '' say Anne decidedly .
`` He want the call but he do preach such gloomy sermon .
Mr. Bell say he 's a minister of the old school , but Mrs. Lynde say there 's nothing whatever the matter with him but indigestion .
His wife be n't a very good cook , it seem , and Mrs. Lynde say that when a man have to eat sour bread two week out of three his theology be bind to get a kink in it somewhere .
Mrs. Allan feel very badly about go away .
She say everybody have be so kind to her since she come here a a bride that she feel a if she be leave lifelong friend .
And then , there 's the baby 's grave , you know .
She say she do n't see how she can go away and leave that ... it be such a little mite of a thing and only three month old , and she say she be afraid it will miss it mother , although she know good and would n't say so to Mr. Allan for anything .
She say she have slip through the birch grove back of the manse nearly every night to the graveyard and sing a little lullaby to it .
She tell me all about it last evening when I be up put some of those early wild rose on Matthew 's grave .
I promise her that as long a I be in Avonlea I would put flower on the baby 's grave and when I be away I felt sure that ... '' `` That I would do it , '' supply Diana heartily .
`` Of course I will .
And I 'll put them on Matthew 's grave too , for your sake , Anne . ''
`` Oh , thank you .
I mean to ask you to if you would .
And on little Hester Gray 's too ?
Please do n't forget hers .
Do you know , I 've think and dream so much about little Hester Gray that she have become strangely real to me .
I think of her , back there in her little garden in that cool , still , green corner ; and I have a fancy that if I could steal back there some spring evening , just at the magic time ` twixt light and dark , and tiptoe so softly up the beech hill that my footstep could not frighten her , I would find the garden just a it use to be , all sweet with June lily and early rose , with the tiny house beyond it all hung with vine ; and little Hester Gray would be there , with her soft eye , and the wind ruffle her dark hair , wander about , put her fingertip under the chin of the lily and whisper secret with the rose ; and I would go forward , oh , so softly , and hold out my hand and say to her , ` Little Hester Gray , wo n't you let me be your playmate , for I love the rose too ? '
And we would sit down on the old bench and talk a little and dream a little , or just be beautifully silent together .
And then the moon would rise and I would look around me ... and there would be no Hester Gray and no little vine-hung house , and no rose ... only an old waste garden star with June lily amid the grass , and the wind sighing , oh , so sorrowfully in the cherry tree .
And I would not know whether it have be real or if I have just imagine it all . ''
Diana crawl up and get her back against the headboard of the bed .
When your companion of twilight hour say such spooky thing it be just as well not to be able to fancy there be anything behind you .
`` I 'm afraid the Improvement Society will go down when you and Gilbert be both go , '' she remark dolefully .
`` Not a bit of fear of it , '' say Anne briskly , come back from dreamland to the affair of practical life .

Look what they be do this summer for their lawn and lane .
Besides , I 'll be watch for hint at Redmond and I 'll write a paper for it next winter and send it over .
Do n't take such a gloomy view of thing , Diana .
And do n't grudge me my little hour of gladness and jubilation now .
Later on , when I have to go away , I 'll feel anything but glad . ''
`` It 's all right for you to be glad ... you 're go to college and you 'll have a jolly time and make heap of lovely new friend . ''
`` I hope I shall make new friend , '' say Anne thoughtfully .
`` The possibility of make new friend help to make life very fascinating .
But no matter how many friend I make they 'll never be a dear to me a the old one ... especially a certain girl with black eye and dimple .
Can you guess who she be , Diana ? ''
`` But there 'll be so many clever girl at Redmond , '' sigh Diana , `` and I 'm only a stupid little country girl who say ' I see ' sometimes ... though I really know good when I stop to think .
Well , of course these past two year have really be too pleasant to last .
I know SOMEBODY who be glad you be go to Redmond anyhow .
Anne , I 'm go to ask you a question ... a serious question .
Do n't be vex and do answer seriously .
Do you care anything for Gilbert ? ''
`` Ever so much a a friend and not a bit in the way you mean , '' say Anne calmly and decidedly ; she also think she be speak sincerely .
Diana sigh .
She wish , somehow , that Anne have answer differently .
`` Do n't you mean EVER to be marry , Anne ? ''
`` Perhaps ... some day ... when I meet the right one , '' say Anne , smile dreamily up at the moonlight .
`` But how can you be sure when you do meet the right one ? ''
persist Diana .
`` Oh , I should know him ... SOMETHING would tell me .
You know what my ideal be , Diana . ''
`` But people 's ideal change sometimes . ''
`` Mine wo n't .
And I COULD N'T care for any man who do n't fulfill it . ''
`` What if you never meet him ? ''
`` Then I shall die an old maid , '' be the cheerful response .
`` I daresay it be n't the hard death by any mean . ''
`` Oh , I suppose the dying would be easy enough ; it 's the live an old maid I should n't like , '' say Diana , with no intention of be humorous .
`` Although I would n't mind be an old maid VERY much if I could be one like Miss Lavendar .
But I never could be .
When I 'm forty-five I 'll be horribly fat .
And while there might be some romance about a thin old maid there could n't possibly be any about a fat one .
Oh , mind you , Nelson Atkins propose to Ruby Gillis three week ago .
Ruby tell me all about it .
She say she never have any intention of take him , because any one who marry him will have to go in with the old folk ; but Ruby say that he make such a perfectly beautiful and romantic proposal that it simply sweep her off her foot .
But she do n't want to do anything rash so she ask for a week to consider ; and two day later she be at a meeting of the Sewing Circle at his mother 's and there be a book call ` The Complete Guide to Etiquette , ' lie on the parlor table .
Ruby say she simply could n't describe her feeling when in a section of it head , ` The Deportment of Courtship and Marriage , ' she find the very proposal Nelson have make , word for word .
She go home and write him a perfectly scathing refusal ; and she say his father and mother have take turn watch him ever since for fear he 'll drown himself in the river ; but Ruby say they need n't be afraid ; for in the Deportment of Courtship and Marriage it tell how a rejected lover should behave and there 's nothing about drown in THAT .
And she say Wilbur Blair be literally pin away for her but she 's perfectly helpless in the matter . ''
Anne make an impatient movement .
`` I hate to say it ... it seem so disloyal ... but , well , I do n't like Ruby Gillis now .
I like her when we go to school and Queen 's together ... though not so well a you and Jane of course .
But this last year at Carmody she seem so different ... so ... so ... '' `` I know , '' nod Diana .
`` It 's the Gillis come out in her ... she ca n't help it .
Mrs. Lynde say that if ever a Gillis girl think about anything but the boy she never show it in her walk and conversation .
She talk about nothing but boy and what compliment they pay her , and how crazy they all be about her at Carmody .
And the strange thing be , they ARE , too ... '' Diana admit this somewhat resentfully .
`` Last night when I saw her in Mr. Blair 's store she whisper to me that she 'd just make a new ` mash . '
I would n't ask her who it be , because I know she be die to BE ask .
Well , it 's what Ruby always want , I suppose .
You remember even when she be little she always say she mean to have dozen of beau when she grow up and have the very gay time she could before she settle down .
She 's so different from Jane , be n't she ?
Jane be such a nice , sensible , lady-like girl . ''
`` Dear old Jane be a jewel , '' agree Anne , `` but , '' she add , lean forward to bestow a tender pat on the plump , dimple little hand hang over her pillow , `` there 's nobody like my own Diana after all .
Do you remember that even we first meet , Diana , and ` swore ' eternal friendship in your garden ?
We 've keep that ` oath , ' I think ... we 've never have a quarrel nor even a coolness .
I shall never forget the thrill that go over me the day you tell me you love me .
I have have such a lonely , starve heart all through my childhood .
I 'm just begin to realize how starved and lonely it really be .
Nobody care anything for me or want to be bother with me .
I should have be miserable if it have n't be for that strange little dream-life of mine , wherein I imagine all the friend and love I crave .
But when I come to Green Gables everything be change .
And then I meet you .
You do n't know what your friendship meant to me .
I want to thank you here and now , dear , for the warm and true affection you 've always give me . ''
`` And always , always will , '' sob Diana .
`` I shall NEVER love anybody ... any GIRL ... half as well a I love you .
And if I ever do marry and have a little girl of my own I 'm go to name her ANNE . ''
XXVII An Afternoon at the Stone House `` Where be you go , all dress up , Anne ? ''
Davy want to know .
`` You look bully in that dress . ''
Anne have come down to dinner in a new dress of pale green muslin ... the first color she have wear since Matthew 's death .
It become her perfectly , bring out all the delicate , flower-like tint of her face and the gloss and burnish of her hair .
`` Davy , how many time have I tell you that you must n't use that word , '' she rebuke .
`` I 'm go to Echo Lodge . ''
`` Take me with you , '' entreat Davy .
`` I would if I be drive .
But I 'm go to walk and it 's too far for your eight-year-old leg .
Besides , Paul be go with me and I fear you do n't enjoy yourself in his company . ''
`` Oh , I like Paul lot well 'n I do , '' say Davy , begin to make fearful inroad into his pudding .
`` Since I 've get pretty good myself I do n't mind his be gooder so much .
If I can keep on I 'll catch up with him some day , both in leg and goodness .
` Sides , Paul 's real nice to u second primer boy in school .
He wo n't let the other big boy meddle with u and he show u lot of game . ''
`` How come Paul to fall into the brook at noon hour yesterday ? ''
ask Anne .
`` I meet him on the playground , such a dripping figure that I send him promptly home for clothes without wait to find out what have happen . ''
`` Well , it be partly a zacksident , '' explain Davy .
`` He stick his head in on purpose but the rest of him fell in zacksidentally .
We be all down at the brook and Prillie Rogerson get mad at Paul about something ... she 's awful mean and horrid anyway , if she IS pretty ... and say that his grandmother put his hair up in curl rag every night .
Paul would n't have mind what she say , I guess , but Gracie Andrews laugh , and Paul get awful red , 'cause Gracie 's his girl , you know .
He 's CLEAN GONE on her ... bring her flower and carry her book as far a the shore road .
He get as red a a beet and say his grandmother do n't do any such thing and his hair be bear curly .
And then he lay down on the bank and stick his head right into the spring to show them .
Oh , it be n't the spring we drink out of ... '' see a horrified look on Marilla 's face ... `` it be the little one low down .
But the bank 's awful slippy and Paul go right in .
I tell you he make a bully splash .
Oh , Anne , Anne , I do n't mean to say that ... it just slip out before I think .
He make a SPLENDID splash .
But he look so funny when he crawl out , all wet and muddy .
The girl laugh more 'n ever , but Gracie do n't laugh .
She look sorry .
Gracie 's a nice girl but she 's get a snub nose .
When I get big enough to have a girl I wo n't have one with a snub nose ... I 'll pick one with a pretty nose like yours , Anne . ''
`` A boy who make such a mess of syrup all over his face when he be eat his pudding will never get a girl to look at him , '' say Marilla severely .
`` But I 'll wash my face before I go court , '' protest Davy , try to improve matter by rub the back of his hand over the smear .
`` And I 'll wash behind my ear too , without be tell .
I remember to this morning , Marilla .
I do n't forget half as often a I do .
But ... '' and Davy sigh ... `` there 's so many corner about a fellow that it 's awful hard to remember them all .
Well , if I ca n't go to Miss Lavendar 's I 'll go over and see Mrs. Harrison .
Mrs. Harrison 's an awful nice woman , I tell you .
She keep a jar of cooky in her pantry a-purpose for little boy , and she always give me the scraping out of a pan she 's mixed up a plum cake in .
A good many plum stick to the side , you see .
Mr. Harrison be always a nice man , but he 's twice a nice since he get marry over again .
I guess get marry make folk nicer .
Why do n't YOU get marry , Marilla ?
I want to know . ''
Marilla 's state of single blessedness have never be a sore point with her , so she answer amiably , with an exchange of significant look with Anne , that she suppose it be because nobody would have her .
`` But maybe you never ask anybody to have you , '' protest Davy .
`` Oh , Davy , '' say Dora primly , shock into speak without be speak to , `` it 's the MEN that have to do the asking . ''
`` I do n't know why they have to do it ALWAYS , '' grumble Davy .
`` Seems to me everything 's put on the men in this world .
Can I have some more pudding , Marilla ? ''
`` You 've have as much a be good for you , '' say Marilla ; but she give him a moderate second help .
`` I wish people could live on pudding .
Why ca n't they , Marilla ?
I want to know . ''
`` Because they 'd soon get tire of it . ''
`` I 'd like to try that for myself , '' say skeptical Davy .
`` But I guess it 's good to have pudding only on fish and company day than none at all .
They never have any at Milty Boulter 's .
Milty say when company come his mother give them cheese and cut it herself ... one little bit apiece and one over for manner . ''
`` If Milty Boulter talk like that about his mother at least you need n't repeat it , '' say Marilla severely .
`` Bless my soul , '' ... Davy have pick this expression up from Mr. Harrison and use it with great gusto ... `` Milty meant it a a compelment .
He 's awful proud of his mother , cause folk say she could scratch a living on a rock . ''
`` I ... I suppose them pesky hen be in my pansy bed again , '' say Marilla , rise and go out hurriedly .
The slandered hen be nowhere near the pansy bed and Marilla do not even glance at it .
Instead , she sit down on the cellar hatch and laugh until she be ashamed of herself .
When Anne and Paul reach the stone house that afternoon they find Miss Lavendar and Charlotta the Fourth in the garden , weeding , rake , clip , and trim a if for dear life .
Miss Lavendar herself , all gay and sweet in the frill and lace she love , drop her shear and run joyously to meet her guest , while Charlotta the Fourth grin cheerfully .
`` Welcome , Anne .
I think you 'd come today .
You belong to the afternoon so it bring you .
Things that belong together be sure to come together .
What a lot of trouble that would save some people if they only know it .
But they do n't ... and so they waste beautiful energy move heaven and earth to bring thing together that DO N'T belong .
And you , Paul ... why , you 've grow !
You 're half a head taller than when you be here before . ''
`` Yes , I 've begin to grow like pigweed in the night , a Mrs. Lynde say , '' say Paul , in frank delight over the fact .
`` Grandma say it 's the porridge take effect at last .
Perhaps it be .
Goodness know ... '' Paul sigh deeply ... `` I 've eat enough to make anyone grow .
I do hope , now that I 've begin , I 'll keep on till I 'm as tall a father .
He be six foot , you know , Miss Lavendar . ''
Yes , Miss Lavendar do know ; the flush on her pretty cheek deepen a little ; she take Paul 's hand on one side and Anne 's on the other and walk to the house in silence .
`` Is it a good day for the echo , Miss Lavendar ? ''
query Paul anxiously .
The day of his first visit have be too windy for echo and Paul have be much disappointed .
`` Yes , just the best kind of a day , '' answer Miss Lavendar , rouse herself from her reverie .
`` But first we be all go to have something to eat .
I know you two folk do n't walk all the way back here through those beechwood without get hungry , and Charlotta the Fourth and I can eat any hour of the day ... we have such obliging appetite .
So we 'll just make a raid on the pantry .
Fortunately it 's lovely and full .
I have a presentiment that I be go to have company today and Charlotta the Fourth and I prepare . ''
`` I think you be one of the people who always have nice thing in their pantry , '' declare Paul .
`` Grandma 's like that too .
But she do n't approve of snack between meal .
I wonder , '' he add meditatively , `` if I OUGHT to eat them away from home when I know she do n't approve . ''
`` Oh , I do n't think she would disapprove after you have have a long walk .
That make a difference , '' say Miss Lavendar , exchange amused glance with Anne over Paul 's brown curl .
`` I suppose that snack ARE extremely unwholesome .
That be why we have them so often at Echo Lodge .
We ... Charlotta the Fourth and I ... live in defiance of every known law of diet .
We eat all sort of indigestible thing whenever we happen to think of it , by day or night ; and we flourish like green bay tree .
We be always intend to reform .
When we read any article in a paper warn u against something we like we cut it out and pin it up on the kitchen wall so that we 'll remember it .
But we never can somehow ... until after we 've go and eat that very thing .
Nothing have ever kill u yet ; but Charlotta the Fourth have be know to have bad dream after we have eat doughnut and mince pie and fruit cake before we go to bed . ''
`` Grandma let me have a glass of milk and a slice of bread and butter before I go to bed ; and on Sunday night she put jam on the bread , '' say Paul .
`` So I 'm always glad when it 's Sunday night ... for more reason than one .
Sunday be a very long day on the shore road .
Grandma say it 's all too short for her and that father never find Sundays tiresome when he be a little boy .
It would n't seem so long if I could talk to my rock people but I never do that because Grandma do n't approve of it on Sundays .
I think a good deal ; but I 'm afraid my thought be worldly .
Grandma say we should never think anything but religious thought on Sundays .
But teacher here say once that every really beautiful thought be religious , no matter what it be about , or what day we think it on .
But I feel sure Grandma think that sermon and Sunday School lesson be the only thing you can think truly religious thought about .
And when it come to a difference of opinion between Grandma and teacher I do n't know what to do .
In my heart '' ... Paul lay his hand on his breast and raise very serious blue eye to Miss Lavendar 's immediately sympathetic face ... `` I agree with teacher .
But then , you see , Grandma have bring father up HER way and make a brilliant success of him ; and teacher have never bring anybody up yet , though she 's help with Davy and Dora .
But you ca n't tell how they 'll turn out till they ARE grow up .
So sometimes I feel a if it might be safe to go by Grandma 's opinion . ''
`` I think it would , '' agree Anne solemnly .
`` Anyway , I daresay that if your Grandma and I both get down to what we really do mean , under our different way of express it , we 'd find out we both mean much the same thing .
You 'd better go by her way of express it , since it 's be the result of experience .
We 'll have to wait until we see how the twin do turn out before we can be sure that my way be equally good . ''
After lunch they go back to the garden , where Paul make the acquaintance of the echo , to his wonder and delight , while Anne and Miss Lavendar sit on the stone bench under the poplar and talk .
`` So you be go away in the fall ? ''
say Miss Lavendar wistfully .
`` I ought to be glad for your sake , Anne ... but I 'm horribly , selfishly sorry .
I shall miss you so much .
Oh , sometimes , I think it be of no use to make friend .
They only go out of your life after awhile and leave a hurt that be bad than the emptiness before they come . ''
`` That sound like something Miss Eliza Andrews might say but never Miss Lavendar , '' say Anne .
`` NOTHING be bad than emptiness ... and I 'm not go out of your life .
There be such thing a letter and vacation .
Dearest , I 'm afraid you 're look a little pale and tire . ''
`` Oh ... hoo ... hoo ... hoo , '' go Paul on the dyke , where he have be make noise diligently ... not all of them melodious in the making , but all come back transmute into the very gold and silver of sound by the fairy alchemists over the river .
Miss Lavendar make an impatient movement with her pretty hand .
`` I 'm just tire of everything ... even of the echo .
There be nothing in my life but echo ... echo of lose hope and dream and joy .
They 're beautiful and mocking .
Oh Anne , it 's horrid of me to talk like this when I have company .
It 's just that I 'm get old and it do n't agree with me .
I know I 'll be fearfully cranky by the time I 'm sixty .
But perhaps all I need be a course of blue pill . ''
At this moment Charlotta the Fourth , who have disappear after lunch , return , and announce that the northeast corner of Mr. John Kimball 's pasture be red with early strawberry , and would n't Miss Shirley like to go and pick some .
`` Early strawberry for tea ! ''
exclaim Miss Lavendar .
`` Oh , I 'm not so old a I think ... and I do n't need a single blue pill !
Girls , when you come back with your strawberry we 'll have tea out here under the silver poplar .
I 'll have it all ready for you with home-grown cream . ''
Anne and Charlotta the Fourth accordingly betook themselves back to Mr. Kimball 's pasture , a green remote place where the air be as soft a velvet and fragrant a a bed of violet and golden a amber .
`` Oh , be n't it sweet and fresh back here ? ''
breathe Anne .
`` I just feel a if I be drink in the sunshine . ''
`` Yes , ma'am , so do I . That 's just exactly how I feel too , ma'am , '' agree Charlotta the Fourth , who would have say precisely the same thing if Anne have remark that she felt like a pelican of the wilderness .
Always after Anne have visit Echo Lodge Charlotta the Fourth mount to her little room over the kitchen and try before her look glass to speak and look and move like Anne .
Charlotta could never flatter herself that she quite succeed ; but practice make perfect , a Charlotta have learn at school , and she fondly hop that in time she might catch the trick of that dainty uplift of chin , that quick , starry outflashing of eye , that fashion of walking a if you be a bough swaying in the wind .
It seem so easy when you watch Anne .
Charlotta the Fourth admire Anne wholeheartedly .
It be not that she think her so very handsome .
Diana Barry 's beauty of crimson cheek and black curl be much more to Charlotta the Fourth 's taste than Anne 's moonshine charm of luminous gray eye and the pale , everchanging rose of her cheek .
`` But I 'd rather look like you than be pretty , '' she tell Anne sincerely .
Anne laugh , sip the honey from the tribute , and cast away the sting .
She be use to take her compliment mixed .
Public opinion never agree on Anne 's look .
People who have hear her call handsome meet her and be disappoint .
People who have hear her call plain saw her and wonder where other people 's eye be .
Anne herself would never believe that she have any claim to beauty .
When she look in the glass all she saw be a little pale face with seven freckle on the nose thereof .
Her mirror never reveal to her the elusive , ever-varying play of feeling that come and go over her feature like a rosy illuminating flame , or the charm of dream and laughter alternating in her big eye .
While Anne be not beautiful in any strictly define sense of the word she possess a certain evasive charm and distinction of appearance that leave beholder with a pleasurable sense of satisfaction in that softly round girlhood of hers , with all it strongly felt potentiality .
Those who know Anne best felt , without realize that they felt it , that her great attraction be the aura of possibility surround her ... the power of future development that be in her .
She seem to walk in an atmosphere of thing about to happen .
As they pick , Charlotta the Fourth confide to Anne her fear regard Miss Lavendar .
The warm-hearted little handmaiden be honestly worry over her adored mistress ' condition .
`` Miss Lavendar be n't well , Miss Shirley , ma'am .
I 'm sure she be n't , though she never complain .
She have n't seem like herself this long while , ma'am ... not since that day you and Paul be here together before .
I feel sure she catch cold that night , ma'am .
After you and him have go she go out and walk in the garden for long after dark with nothing but a little shawl on her .
There be a lot of snow on the walk and I feel sure she get a chill , ma'am .
Ever since then I 've notice her acting tired and lonesome like .
She do n't seem to take an interest in anything , ma'am .
She never pretend company 's coming , nor fix up for it , nor nothing , ma'am .
It 's only when you come she seem to chirk up a bit .
And the bad sign of all , Miss Shirley , ma'am ... '' Charlotta the Fourth lower her voice a if she be about to tell some exceedingly weird and awful symptom indeed ... `` be that she never get cross now when I break thing .
Why , Miss Shirley , ma'am , yesterday I bruk her green and yaller bowl that 's always stand on the bookcase .
Her grandmother bring it out from England and Miss Lavendar be awful choice of it .
I be dust it just a careful , Miss Shirley , ma'am , and it slip out , so fashion , afore I could grab holt of it , and bruk into about forty millyun piece .
I tell you I be sorry and scar .
I think Miss Lavendar would scold me awful , ma'am ; and I 'd ruther she have than take it the way she do .
She just come in and hardly look at it and say , ` It 's no matter , Charlotta .
Take up the piece and throw them away . '
Just like that , Miss Shirley , ma'am ... ` take up the piece and throw them away , ' a if it be n't her grandmother 's bowl from England .
Oh , she be n't well and I feel awful bad about it .
She 's get nobody to look after her but me . ''
Charlotta the Fourth 's eye brim up with tear .
Anne pat the little brown paw hold the cracked pink cup sympathetically .
`` I think Miss Lavendar need a change , Charlotta .
She stay here alone too much .
Ca n't we induce her to go away for a little trip ? ''
Charlotta shake her head , with it rampant bow , disconsolately .
`` I do n't think so , Miss Shirley , ma'am .
Miss Lavendar hat visit .
She 's only get three relation she ever visit and she say she just go to see them a a family duty .
Last time when she come home she say she be n't go to visit for family duty no more .
` I 've come home in love with loneliness , Charlotta , ' she say to me , ` and I never want to stray from my own vine and fig tree again .
My relation try so hard to make an old lady of me and it have a bad effect on me . '
Just like that , Miss Shirley , ma'am .
` It have a very bad effect on me . '
So I do n't think it would do any good to coax her to go visiting . ''
`` We must see what can be do , '' say Anne decidedly , a she put the last possible berry in her pink cup .
`` Just as soon a I have my vacation I 'll come through and spend a whole week with you .
We 'll have a picnic every day and pretend all sort of interesting thing , and see if we ca n't cheer Miss Lavendar up . ''
`` That will be the very thing , Miss Shirley , ma'am , '' exclaim Charlotta the Fourth in rapture .
She be glad for Miss Lavendar 's sake and for her own too .
With a whole week in which to study Anne constantly she would surely be able to learn how to move and behave like her .
When the girl get back to Echo Lodge they find that Miss Lavendar and Paul have carry the little square table out of the kitchen to the garden and have everything ready for tea .
Nothing ever taste so delicious a those strawberry and cream , eat under a great blue sky all curdle over with fluffy little white cloud , and in the long shadow of the wood with it lispings and it murmuring .
After tea Anne help Charlotta wash the dish in the kitchen , while Miss Lavendar sit on the stone bench with Paul and hear all about his rock people .
She be a good listener , this sweet Miss Lavendar , but just at the last it strike Paul that she have suddenly lose interest in the Twin Sailors .
`` Miss Lavendar , why do you look at me like that ? ''
he ask gravely .
`` How do I look , Paul ? ''
`` Just a if you be look through me at somebody I put you in mind of , '' say Paul , who have such occasional flash of uncanny insight that it be n't quite safe to have secret when he be about .
`` You do put me in mind of somebody I know long ago , '' say Miss Lavendar dreamily .
`` When you be young ? ''
`` Yes , when I be young .
Do I seem very old to you , Paul ? ''
`` Do you know , I ca n't make up my mind about that , '' say Paul confidentially .
`` Your hair look old ... I never know a young person with white hair .
But your eye be as young a my beautiful teacher 's when you laugh .
I tell you what , Miss Lavendar '' ... Paul 's voice and face be as solemn a a judge 's ... `` I think you would make a splendid mother .
You have just the right look in your eye ... the look my little mother always have .
I think it 's a pity you have n't any boy of your own . ''
`` I have a little dream boy , Paul . ''
`` Oh , have you really ?
How old be he ? ''
`` About your age I think .
He ought to be old because I dream him long before you be bear .
But I 'll never let him get any old than eleven or twelve ; because if I do some day he might grow up altogether and then I 'd lose him . ''
`` I know , '' nod Paul .
`` That 's the beauty of dream-people ... they stay any age you want them .
You and my beautiful teacher and me myself be the only folk in the world that I know of that have dream-people .
Is n't it funny and nice we should all know each other ?
But I guess that kind of people always find each other out .
Grandma never have dream-people and Mary Joe think I 'm wrong in the upper story because I have them .
But I think it 's splendid to have them .
YOU know , Miss Lavendar .
Tell me all about your little dream-boy . ''
`` He have blue eye and curly hair .
He steal in and wakens me with a kiss every morning .
Then all day he play here in the garden ... and I play with him .
Such game a we have .
We run race and talk with the echo ; and I tell him story .
And when twilight come ... '' `` I know , '' interrupt Paul eagerly .
`` He come and sits beside you ... SO ... because of course at twelve he 'd be too big to climb into your lap ... and lay his head on your shoulder ... SO ... and you put your arm about him and hold him tight , tight , and rest your cheek on his head ... yes , that 's the very way .
Oh , you DO know , Miss Lavendar . ''
Anne find the two of them there when she come out of the stone house , and something in Miss Lavendar 's face make her hate to disturb them .
`` I 'm afraid we must go , Paul , if we want to get home before dark .
Miss Lavendar , I 'm go to invite myself to Echo Lodge for a whole week pretty soon . ''
`` If you come for a week I 'll keep you for two , '' threaten Miss Lavendar .
XXVIII The Prince Comes Back to the Enchanted Palace The last day of school come and go .
A triumphant `` semi-annual examination '' be hold and Anne 's pupil acquit themselves splendidly .
At the close they give her an address and a writing desk .
All the girl and lady present cry , and some of the boy have it cast up to them later on that they cry too , although they always deny it .
Mrs. Harmon Andrews , Mrs. Peter Sloane , and Mrs. William Bell walk home together and talk thing over .
`` I do think it be such a pity Anne be leave when the child seem so much attach to her , '' sigh Mrs. Peter Sloane , who have a habit of sigh over everything and even finish off her joke that way .
`` To be sure , '' she add hastily , `` we all know we 'll have a good teacher next year too . ''
`` Jane will do her duty , I 've no doubt , '' say Mrs. Andrews rather stiffly .
`` I do n't suppose she 'll tell the child quite so many fairy tale or spend so much time roam about the wood with them .
But she have her name on the Inspector 's Roll of Honor and the Newbridge people be in a terrible state over her leaving . ''
`` I 'm real glad Anne be go to college , '' say Mrs. Bell .
`` She have always want it and it will be a splendid thing for her . ''
`` Well , I do n't know . ''
Mrs. Andrews be determine not to agree fully with anybody that day .
`` I do n't see that Anne need any more education .
She 'll probably be marry Gilbert Blythe , if his infatuation for her last till he get through college , and what good will Latin and Greek do her then ?
If they teach you at college how to manage a man there might be some sense in her go . ''
Mrs. Harmon Andrews , so Avonlea gossip whisper , have never learn how to manage her `` man , '' and a a result the Andrews household be not exactly a model of domestic happiness .
`` I see that the Charlottetown call to Mr. Allan be up before the Presbytery , '' say Mrs. Bell .
`` That mean we 'll be lose him soon , I suppose . ''

He go to church every Sunday and have subscribe to the salary . ''

She have have her reward .

`` Yes , I believe you be .

Would she not ?
Oh , indeed she would !

XXIX Poetry and Prose For the next month Anne live in what , for Avonlea , might be call a whirl of excitement .

`` When 's the wedding to be ? ''
she ask after a pause .
`` The last Wednesday in August .

There be more romance in the world than that which have fall to the share of the middle-aged lover of the stone house .
Anne stumble suddenly on it one evening when she go over to Orchard Slope by the wood cut and come out into the Barry garden .
Diana Barry and Fred Wright be stand together under the big willow .
Diana be lean against the gray trunk , her lash cast down on very crimson cheek .
One hand be hold by Fred , who stand with his face bent toward her , stammer something in low earnest tone .

`` Diana and Fred be in love with each other , '' she gasp .

Anne , of late , have not be without her suspicion that Diana be prove false to the melancholy Byronic hero of her early dream .

This be succeed by a queer , little lonely feel ... a if , somehow , Diana have go forward into a new world , shut a gate behind her , leave Anne on the outside .

`` And I 'm afraid that this ca n't help make some difference between Diana and me .
I 'm sure I ca n't tell her all my secret after this ... she might tell Fred .
And what CAN she see in Fred ?
He 's very nice and jolly ... but he 's just Fred Wright . ''
It be always a very puzzling question ... what can somebody see in somebody else ?
But how fortunate after all that it be so , for if everybody saw alike ... well , in that case , a the old Indian say , `` Everybody would want my squaw . ''
It be plain that Diana DID see something in Fred Wright , however Anne 's eye might be holden .
Diana come to Green Gables the next evening , a pensive , shy young lady , and tell Anne the whole story in the dusky seclusion of the east gable .
Both girl cry and kiss and laugh .
`` I 'm so happy , '' say Diana , `` but it do seem ridiculous to think of me be engage . ''
`` What be it really like to be engage ? ''

`` I do n't mean THAT ... it 's so hard to explain .
Never mind , you 'll understand sometime , when your own turn come . ''
`` Bless you , dear of Dianas , I understand now .
What be an imagination for if not to enable you to peep at life through other people 's eye ? ''

Anne have no sooner utter the phrase , `` home o'dreams , '' than it captivate her fancy and she immediately begin the erection of one of her own .
It be , of course , tenant by an ideal master , dark , proud , and melancholy ; but oddly enough , Gilbert Blythe persist in hang about too , help her arrange picture , lay out garden , and accomplish sundry other task which a proud and melancholy hero evidently consider beneath his dignity .

`` I suppose , Anne , you must think it 's funny I should like Fred so well when he 's so different from the kind of man I 've always say I would marry ... the tall , slender kind ?

But after all that 's good than one of u be short and fat and the other tall and lean , like Morgan Sloane and his wife .

But then Diana think so too , once .
I 've hear her say time and again she 'd never get engage any poky commonplace way ... he 'd HAVE to do something splendid to win her .
But she have change .
Perhaps I 'll change too .
But I wo n't ... and I 'm determined I wo n't .
Oh , I think these engagement be dreadfully unsettling thing when they happen to your intimate friend . ''
XXX A Wedding at the Stone House The last week in August come .
Miss Lavendar be to be marry in it .
Two week later Anne and Gilbert would leave for Redmond College .
In a week 's time Mrs. Rachel Lynde would move to Green Gables and set up her lares and penates in the erstwhile spare room , which be already prepared for her coming .
She have sell all her superfluous household plenishings by auction and be at present revel in the congenial occupation of help the Allans pack up .
Mr. Allan be to preach his farewell sermon the next Sunday .
The old order be change rapidly to give place to the new , a Anne felt with a little sadness thread all her excitement and happiness .
`` Changes ai n't totally pleasant but they 're excellent thing , '' say Mr. Harrison philosophically .
`` Two year be about long enough for thing to stay exactly the same .
If they stay put any long they might grow mossy . ''
Mr. Harrison be smoke on his veranda .
His wife have self-sacrificingly tell that he might smoke in the house if he take care to sit by an open window .
Mr. Harrison reward this concession by go outdoors altogether to smoke in fine weather , and so mutual goodwill reign .
Anne have come over to ask Mrs. Harrison for some of her yellow dahlia .
She and Diana be go through to Echo Lodge that even to help Miss Lavendar and Charlotta the Fourth with their final preparation for the morrow 's bridal .
Miss Lavendar herself never have dahlia ; she do not like them and they would not have suit the fine retirement of her old-fashioned garden .
But flower of any kind be rather scarce in Avonlea and the neighboring district that summer , thanks to Uncle Abe 's storm ; and Anne and Diana think that a certain old cream-colored stone jug , usually keep sacred to doughnuts , brim over with yellow dahlia , would be just the thing to set in a dim angle of the stone house stair , against the dark background of red hall paper .
`` I s ` pose you 'll be start off for college in a fortnight 's time ? ''
continued Mr. Harrison .
`` Well , we 're go to miss you an awful lot , Emily and me .
To be sure , Mrs. Lynde 'll be over there in your place .
There ai n't nobody but a substitute can be find for them . ''
The irony of Mr. Harrison 's tone be quite untransferable to paper .
In spite of his wife 's intimacy with Mrs. Lynde , the best that could be say of the relationship between her and Mr. Harrison even under the new regime , be that they preserve an arm neutrality .
`` Yes , I 'm go , '' say Anne .
`` I 'm very glad with my head ... and very sorry with my heart . ''
`` I s ` pose you 'll be scoop up all the honor that be lie round loose at Redmond . ''
`` I may try for one or two of them , '' confess Anne , `` but I do n't care so much for thing like that a I do two year ago .
What I want to get out of my college course be some knowledge of the best way of live life and do the most and best with it .
I want to learn to understand and help other people and myself . ''
Mr. Harrison nod .
`` That 's the idea exactly .
That 's what college ought to be for , instead of for turn out a lot of B.A . 's , so chock full of book-learning and vanity that there ai n't room for anything else .
You 're all right .
College wo n't be able to do you much harm , I reckon . ''
Diana and Anne drive over to Echo Lodge after tea , take with them all the flowery spoil that several predatory expedition in their own and their neighbor ' garden have yield .
They find the stone house agog with excitement .
Charlotta the Fourth be fly around with such vim and briskness that her blue bow seem really to possess the power of be everywhere at once .
Like the helmet of Navarre , Charlotta 's blue bow wave ever in the thickest of the fray .
`` Praise be to goodness you 've come , '' she say devoutly , `` for there 's heap of thing to do ... and the frosting on that cake WO N'T harden ... and there 's all the silver to be rub up yet ... and the horsehair trunk to be pack ... and the rooster for the chicken salad be run out there beyant the henhouse yet , crow , Miss Shirley , ma'am .
And Miss Lavendar ai n't to be trust to do a thing .
I be thankful when Mr. Irving come a few minute ago and take her off for a walk in the wood .
Courting 's all right in it place , Miss Shirley , ma'am , but if you try to mix it up with cooking and scour everything 's spoil .
That 's MY opinion , Miss Shirley , ma'am . ''
Anne and Diana work so heartily that by ten o'clock even Charlotta the Fourth be satisfy .
She braid her hair in innumerable plait and take her weary little bone off to bed .
`` But I 'm sure I sha n't sleep a blessed wink , Miss Shirley , ma'am , for fear that something 'll go wrong at the last minute ... the cream wo n't whip ... or Mr. Irving 'll have a stroke and not be able to come . ''
`` He be n't in the habit of have stroke , be he ? ''
ask Diana , the dimpled corner of her mouth twitching .
To Diana , Charlotta the Fourth be , if not exactly a thing of beauty , certainly a joy forever .
`` They 're not thing that go by habit , '' say Charlotta the Fourth with dignity .
`` They just HAPPEN ... and there you be .
ANYBODY can have a stroke .
You do n't have to learn how .
Mr. Irving look a lot like an uncle of mine that have one once just a he be sit down to dinner one day .
But maybe everything 'll go all right .
In this world you 've just get to hope for the best and prepare for the bad and take whatever God send . ''
`` The only thing I 'm worried about be that it wo n't be fine tomorrow , '' say Diana .
`` Uncle Abe predict rain for the middle of the week , and ever since the big storm I ca n't help believe there 's a good deal in what Uncle Abe say . ''
Anne , who know good than Diana just how much Uncle Abe have to do with the storm , be not much disturb by this .
She sleep the sleep of the just and weary , and be rouse at an unearthly hour by Charlotta the Fourth .
`` Oh , Miss Shirley , ma'am , it 's awful to call you so early , '' come wail through the keyhole , `` but there 's so much to do yet ... and oh , Miss Shirley , ma'am , I 'm skeered it 's go to rain and I wish you 'd get up and tell me you think it ai n't . ''
Anne fly to the window , hop against hope that Charlotta the Fourth be say this merely by way of rouse her effectually .
But ala , the morning do look unpropitious .
Below the window Miss Lavendar 's garden , which should have be a glory of pale virgin sunshine , lay dim and windless ; and the sky over the fir be dark with moody cloud .
`` Is n't it too mean ! ''
say Diana .
`` We must hope for the best , '' say Anne determinedly .
`` If it only do n't actually rain , a cool , pearly gray day like this would really be nice than hot sunshine . ''
`` But it will rain , '' mourn Charlotta , creep into the room , a figure of fun , with her many braid wind about her head , the end , tie up with white thread , stick out in all direction .
`` It 'll hold off till the last minute and then pour cat and dog .
And all the folk will get sopping ... and track mud all over the house ... and they wo n't be able to be marry under the honeysuckle ... and it 's awful unlucky for no sun to shine on a bride , say what you will , Miss Shirley , ma'am .
I know thing be go too well to last . ''
Charlotta the Fourth seem certainly to have borrow a leaf out of Miss Eliza Andrews ' book .
It do not rain , though it keep on look a if it mean to .
By noon the room be decorate , the table beautifully lay ; and upstairs be wait a bride , `` adorn for her husband . ''
`` You do look sweet , '' say Anne rapturously .
`` Lovely , '' echo Diana .
`` Everything 's ready , Miss Shirley , ma'am , and nothing dreadful have happen YET , '' be Charlotta 's cheerful statement a she betook herself to her little back room to dress .
Out come all the braid ; the resultant rampant crinkliness be plait into two tail and tie , not with two bow alone , but with four , of brand-new ribbon , brightly blue .
The two upper bow rather give the impression of overgrown wing sprout from Charlotta 's neck , somewhat after the fashion of Raphael 's cherub .
But Charlotta the Fourth think them very beautiful , and after she have rustle into a white dress , so stiffly starch that it could stand alone , she survey herself in her glass with great satisfaction ... a satisfaction which last until she go out in the hall and catch a glimpse through the spare room door of a tall girl in some softly cling gown , pin white , star-like flower on the smooth ripple of her ruddy hair .
`` Oh , I 'll NEVER be able to look like Miss Shirley , '' think poor Charlotta despairingly .
`` You just have to be bear so , I guess ... do n't seem 's if any amount of practice could give you that AIR . ''
By one o'clock the guest have come , include Mr. and Mrs. Allan , for Mr. Allan be to perform the ceremony in the absence of the Grafton minister on his vacation .
There be no formality about the marriage .
Miss Lavendar come down the stair to meet her bridegroom at the foot , and a he take her hand she lift her big brown eye to his with a look that make Charlotta the Fourth , who intercept it , feel queer than ever .
They go out to the honeysuckle arbor , where Mr. Allan be await them .
The guest group themselves a they please .
Anne and Diana stand by the old stone bench , with Charlotta the Fourth between them , desperately clutch their hand in her cold , tremulous little paw .
Mr. Allan open his blue book and the ceremony proceed .
Just a Miss Lavendar and Stephen Irving be pronounce man and wife a very beautiful and symbolic thing happen .
The sun suddenly burst through the gray and pour a flood of radiance on the happy bride .
Instantly the garden be alive with dance shadow and flicker light .
`` What a lovely omen , '' think Anne , a she run to kiss the bride .
Then the three girl leave the rest of the guest laugh around the bridal pair while they fly into the house to see that all be in readiness for the feast .
`` Thanks be to goodness , it 's over , Miss Shirley , ma'am , '' breathe Charlotta the Fourth , `` and they 're married safe and sound , no matter what happen now .
The bag of rice be in the pantry , ma'am , and the old shoe be behind the door , and the cream for whip be on the sullar step . ''
At half past two Mr. and Mrs. Irving leave , and everybody go to Bright River to see them off on the afternoon train .
As Miss Lavendar ... I beg her pardon , Mrs. Irving ... step from the door of her old home Gilbert and the girl throw the rice and Charlotta the Fourth hurl an old shoe with such excellent aim that she strike Mr. Allan squarely on the head .
But it be reserve for Paul to give the pretty send-off .
He pop out of the porch ring furiously a huge old brass dinner bell which have adorn the dining room mantel .
Paul 's only motive be to make a joyful noise ; but a the clangor die away , from point and curve and hill across the river come the chime of `` fairy wedding bell , '' ring clearly , sweetly , faintly and more faint , a if Miss Lavendar 's beloved echo be bid her greeting and farewell .
And so , amid this benediction of sweet sound , Miss Lavendar drive away from the old life of dream and make-believe to a full life of reality in the busy world beyond .
Two hour later Anne and Charlotta the Fourth come down the lane again .
Gilbert have go to West Grafton on an errand and Diana have to keep an engagement at home .
Anne and Charlotta have come back to put thing in order and lock up the little stone house .
The garden be a pool of late golden sunshine , with butterfly hover and bee boom ; but the little house have already that indefinable air of desolation which always follow a festivity .
`` Oh dear me , do n't it look lonesome ? ''
sniffed Charlotta the Fourth , who have be cry all the way home from the station .
`` A wedding ai n't much cheerfuller than a funeral after all , when it 's all over , Miss Shirley , ma'am . ''
A busy evening follow .
The decoration have to be remove , the dish wash , the uneaten delicacy pack into a basket for the delectation of Charlotta the Fourth 's young brother at home .
Anne would not rest until everything be in apple-pie order ; after Charlotta have go home with her plunder Anne go over the still room , feel like one who tread alone some banquet hall desert , and close the blind .
Then she lock the door and sit down under the silver poplar to wait for Gilbert , feel very tired but still unweariedly think `` long , long thought . ''
`` What be you think of , Anne ? ''
ask Gilbert , come down the walk .
He have leave his horse and buggy out at the road .
`` Of Miss Lavendar and Mr. Irving , '' answer Anne dreamily .
`` Is n't it beautiful to think how everything have turn out ... how they have come together again after all the year of separation and misunderstanding ? ''
`` Yes , it 's beautiful , '' say Gilbert , look steadily down into Anne 's uplifted face , `` but would n't it have be more beautiful still , Anne , if there have be NO separation or misunderstanding ... if they have come hand in hand all the way through life , with no memory behind them but those which belong to each other ? ''
For a moment Anne 's heart flutter queerly and for the first time her eye falter under Gilbert 's gaze and a rosy flush stain the paleness of her face .
It be a if a veil that have hung before her inner consciousness have be lift , give to her view a revelation of unsuspected feeling and reality .
Perhaps , after all , romance do not come into one 's life with pomp and blare , like a gay knight rid down ; perhaps it creep to one 's side like an old friend through quiet way ; perhaps it reveal itself in seem prose , until some sudden shaft of illumination flung athwart it page betray the rhythm and the music , perhaps ... perhaps ... love unfold naturally out of a beautiful friendship , a a golden-hearted rise slip from it green sheath .
Then the veil drop again ; but the Anne who walk up the dark lane be not quite the same Anne who have drive gaily down it the evening before .
The page of girlhood have be turn , a by an unseen finger , and the page of womanhood be before her with all it charm and mystery , it pain and gladness .
Gilbert wisely say nothing more ; but in his silence he read the history of the next four year in the light of Anne 's remembered blush .
Four year of earnest , happy work ... and then the guerdon of a useful knowledge gain and a sweet heart win .
Behind them in the garden the little stone house brood among the shadow .
It be lonely but not forsake .
It have not yet do with dream and laughter and the joy of life ; there be to be future summer for the little stone house ; meanwhile , it could wait .
